Approval
This master thesis was prepared over a period of five months at the Department of Wind and Energy
Systems, DTU Wind, at the Technical University of Denmark (DTU), in partial fulfillment for the
degree Master of Science in Engineering, M.Sc. in Sustainable Energy (Solar Energy). The thesis
was conducted in collaboration with Green Power Denmark and corresponds to 30 ECTS points
of study. The thesis was carried out under the supervision of Guangya Yang, Senior Researcher,
Department of Wind and Energy Systems and Konrad Sundsgaard, Industrial PhD student at Green
Power Denmark.

ii
Modelling and optimal planning of digitized and automated secondary substation
15-04-2023
==PAGE:5==
Abstract
In recent years, the digitization of distribution grids have accelerated intensely, leading to a signifi-
cant increase in the number of digitized and automated secondary substations. The benefits of these
intelligent elements in the distribution grid are widely accepted by both industry and academia.
Further the best practices in terms of optimal planning and placement of the digitized and auto-
mated secondary substations in the distribution networks are being extensively investigated. With
the advent of renewable renewable energy sources and the major transition taking place in the en-
ergy sector to green energy, more and more renewable energy sources are being integrated at the
distribution grid level. However, future projections for the distribution grids predict an unequal
integration of these renewable resources at the distribution level and this pose as a challenge to
the Distribution System Operators (DSOs) in a way from reliability perspective. This necessitates
investigation towards new and improved strategies for optimal placement and rollout of digitized
secondary substations in distribution networks. In this thesis, an investigation on optimal location
for placement of digitized secondary substations in various distribution grid types and further pri-
oritization of grids that benefit from a digitized secondary substation placement is conducted. The
study primarily focuses on system reliability as the key parameter for investigating the placement
of digitized secondary substations in the feeder networks of the modelled distinct distribution grid
types.
To investigate the impact of optimally placing digitized secondary substations in a distribution
grid, a model of digitized secondary substation is created in PowerFactory. The model is capable
of performing fault isolation and optimal network reconfiguration through fast remote-controlled
switching. Further, for analysing the optimal location and the benefit of placing a digitized sec-
ondary substation from a reliability perspective in the distribution grid, three distinct distribution
grid types (city, suburban and energy island) are modeled in PowerFactory. The grid types vary
from one another in the load levels at the substations, renewable energy source mix and the MV
cable types that they include. Additionally, network variation scenarios are created for each dis-
tinct grid type by varying the location of the load and generation units that constitute a grid type.
The modelling and development of these grid type scenarios are performed in a Python computing
platform and the workflow for this is presented as a part of this thesis.
The modeled grid type scenarios are subjected to an optimization algorithm developed in Python.
The optimization algorithm digitizes each secondary substation in the feeder network of the grid
type scenarios and investigates the substation location that provides the least Energy not Supplied
(ENS) indice on performing reliability assessment on the grid type scenarios. This way the al-
gorithm determines the optimal location for digitized secondary substation placement, as well as
the benefits of such placement in terms of minimization of energy interruptions from a reliability
standpoint. Ranking of the grid type scenarios is then performed to propose a prioritization for the
grid types based on the benefits they can avail from placing a digitized secondary substation.
Modelling and optimal planning of digitized and automated secondary substation
iii
==PAGE:6==
Executive Summary
This thesis investigates optimal placement locations for digitized secondary substations in distribu-
tion grids and proposes a prioritization framework based on reliability benefits. The study begins
by modeling a digitized secondary substation template with fault detection and optimal network
reconfiguration capabilities in PowerFactory, utilizing remote-controlled switching capabilities. A
test distribution grid is chosen and modeled, and the reliability assessment toolbox in PowerFac-
tory is examined. The study also explores basic scripting for PowerFactory in Python to configure
and access it effectively.
Three distribution grid types are analyzed for optimal digitized secondary substation placement
and prioritized based on reliability benefits. The first grid is a weak urban city grid with high load
concentration and high MV line failure rates. The second is a strong suburban grid with medium
load concentration and low MV line failure rates. The third is a hypothesized energy island grid
with medium load concentration, low MV line failure rates, and high renewable energy penetration.
PowerFactory’s time characteristics functionality for elements is incorporated to create hourly load
and generation profiles for the grid types. All three grid types are modeled through a combination
of manual modeling and Python coding on the chosen test distribution grid. Network variations
of the grid types are created by placing load and generation units at various locations within the
feeders to create scenarios to investigate optimal placement of digitized secondary substations in
them.
The study develops two optimization algorithms to improve system reliability by minimizing En-
ergy Not Supplied (ENS) through optimal placement of digitized secondary substations in distinct
grid type scenarios. The first algorithm, Particle Swarm Optimization (PSO), digitizes each sec-
ondary substation individually in the feeder and uses swarm intelligence to find the optimal location.
However, the PSO algorithm is found to be inefficient and time-consuming for smaller grids, so
the study develops a conventional optimization technique that digitizes each secondary substation
sequentially and identifies the optimal location by comparing ENS. The conventional optimization
technique is ultimately used to determine the optimal placement of digitized secondary substations
in the modeled grid scenarios.
The results indicate that the optimal location to place a digitized secondary substation within a
feeder is at the substation with an open tie point connection to its neighboring feeder. Furthermore,
when ranking the maximum benefit in terms of ENS reduction (%), city-type grids exhibit the
highest benefit from placing a digitized secondary substation. Additionally, a sensitivity analysis
of the optimal location for placement of digitized secondary substations based on mixed MV cable
failure frequencies is conducted by mixing the cable types in the feeder network of scenarios and
it shows no specific grid type that exhibits the maximum benefit in terms of ENS reduction (%).
Instead, the ranking indicates that the benefit in ENS reduction (%) groups around specific feeder
types chosen for digitized secondary substation placement.
This study’s key observations are limited to a few important drivers and require future studies to
consider other influencing factors that may affect the optimal placement of digitized secondary
substations. Sensitivity analysis suggests that detailed studies on feeder characteristics, including
feeder length, cable length, substation layout, and open tie points should investigate their correla-
tion with ENS reduction. Furthermore, looking at ENS for a grid is only part of the problem, and
the current optimization approach has limitations in its capability. Therefore, the combination of
multiple criteria, such as state estimation and data collection, is essential to obtain a more realistic
and accurate insight into the optimal planning and rollout of digitized secondary substations.

CHAPTER 1
Current state of power distribution
networks: challenges and opportunities
Electric distribution systems are an integral part of the electric power system network as they play
the key role of delivering electricity to the customers [1]. The distribution system operators (DSOs)
are in charge for ensuring the continuity of electricity supply to the customers [2]. Their core
objectives include maintaining continuity of supply, power quality, low variations in magnitude of
voltage and restrict the transients in voltage and current to minimal.
The quality of power supply is affected by a lot of factors, the most important ones to be addressed
include the continuity of supply, voltage quality and network losses. According to [3], DSOs are
encountering a range of challenges in their efforts to guarantee the cost-effective, dependable, and
secure development and operation of their networks [3]. These challenges include:
• A steady increase in the proportion of distributed renewable generation being integrated into the
distribution grid.
• Increasing number of active customers participating in demand response programs and adopting
electric mobility.
In the recent decades with ongoing energy transition, increase in Distributed Energy Resources
(DER) connected to the distribution grid and advent of bidirectional digital communication tech-
nologies such as smart metering, DSOs have been able to manage electricity distribution. The re-
liability indices such as the System Average Interruption Duration Index (SAIDI) and the System
Average Interruption Frequency Index (SAIFI) which render the duration and frequency of supply
interruptions have been significantly decreased by 31% and 25% respectively since 2015 in all
European countries due to continuous efforts from DSOs to improve service quality [3]. However,
[3] concludes that the DSOs have been capable of dealing with the rising volumes of distributed
generation owing to the strength of the grid, but over time, further growth in distributed generation
will challenge DSOs towards the smooth operation of grid infrastructure. As a result, the foregoing
DSOs who are responsible for ensuring this continuity of supply are facing immense pressure to
cater to the rising electricity demands.
Europe’s distribution system will need to adapt its role to keep pace with the transformation of the
energy world and with changing customer needs. It is inevitable that DSOs will need to harness
volumes of flexibility in the power system to ensure system stability as huge investments would
be required to keep maintaining high standards of quality of supply and further enabling long-term
decarbonisation goals. In addition, DSOs also want to make better use of their assets and manage
Modelling and optimal planning of digitized and automated secondary substation
1
==PAGE:16==
the grid in a more intelligent way.
Traditionally, it was much easier for distribution networks to perform services which include
maintaining voltage quality, minimising the distribution network losses, annulling electrical dis-
turbances and many more. However, with emergence of distributed generation resources, these
services got much more complex. This led to development of smart grids which use a two-way
flow of electricity and information for an advanced energy delivery network [4]. One of the func-
tionalities of smart grid include smart protection system that ensure the system reliability and pro-
vides protection against potential failures that could happen in the grid. In the event of a failure,
the grid needs to quickly locate and identify the failure to avoid further cascading events and this
process becomes more and more complex when a distribution grid comprises various distributed
generation resources. One way to endure such failure scenarios would be to establish self-healing
capabilities for the distribution grid. With appropriate network system reconfiguration in the event
of a failure, the impact of such failure can be isolated, and the service can be restored quicker,
thereby reducing the outage time [4].
As per [5], 22% of initial causes of unplanned power outages are a result of system equipment
failures. Power outages affect both the electricity consumers and the utility companies responsible
for power distribution. Electric supply companies incur energy sales loss and other operation and
maintenance expenditure for service restoration [5]. In consequence, the reliability-based design
of distribution systems needs to aim at minimizing the frequency and duration of electric supply in-
terruptions. Further, the reconfiguration methodology under fault conditions requires rapid action
for service restoration, as a result heuristic techniques are commonly used [5]. Since reliability
indices provide the primary information to base the design and planning of electrical distribution
systems by the utilities, correct and appropriate methods are to be adopted to calculate and evaluate
these indices. Most utilities usually focus on historical data to find ways to improve the reliabil-
ity of areas with poor reliability, however, it gets complicated and impossible with large amounts
of components in the system, leading to a lot more possible and impossible scenarios and this
necessitates computer simulations with improved capabilities to handle complex grids [6]. The
reliability assessment module offered by ‘DIgSILENT PowerFactory’, provides calculated results
on expected interruption frequency, annual interruption costs and possibility to compare alternate
network designs [6]. Failures and loads can be modelled for component elements of the electric
network within PowerFactory, the combination of one or more simultaneous faults and specify load
conditions are used to create system state, which are then simulated and analysed by Failure Effect
Analysis (FEA) module within reliability assessment function. The basic functionality of FEA is
to determine the information on system faults that are leading to interruption. With the help of a
scripting software such as ‘Python’, the complex process of inputting failure models, load models
for element components in the electrical design can be automated [7].
In a distribution system, medium to low voltage substations serve a crucial function beyond sim-
ply stepping down to low voltage levels. They also play a key role in ensuring the continuity of
power supply to connected customers. Additionally, these substations distribute energy throughout
the medium voltage network, allowing for topology changes during both planned and unplanned
outages. For the realization of smart grids, secondary substations need to evolve to improve the
continuity of supply, and this necessitates sufficient automated features. Traditionally, the focus on
automation centred around improving the operational capabilities of transmission networks where
the number of elements controlled is much lower and the customers connected to each node are
higher [8]. However, introduction of large volumes of distributed generation resources, heat pumps
and e-mobility call for automation in distribution grid. Digital and smart secondary substations
with a wide range of functionalities such as intelligent fault detection (short circuit indicators) and
isolation, remote controlled sectionalizing switches, data acquisition equipment can provide the
2
Modelling and optimal planning of digitized and automated secondary substation
==PAGE:17==
necessary services for enhancing the operation of distribution [9].
Figure 1.1: Traditional fault isolation process[9]
Conventionally, the process of fault identification and then segmentation is achieved through an
iterative manual process where switches are operated at different segment by the maintenance
crew as shown in figure 1.1. The figure shows two MV feeders connected to the same HV/MV
substation through a normally open switch (Sab). Also, each feeder here has six MV/LV secondary
substations. So, if a fault was to occur somewhere between TSa2 and TSa3, Sa1 trips to isolate
the fault. Further upon the arrival of the maintenance crew, they open the manual switch to the
right of Tsa4 and close Sa1 and that trips Sa1. The crew then closes the Sab (open tie point switch)
and the switch to the left of TSa4, so that the service is restored for Tsa4-Tsa6. Similarly, the crew
would repeat this process of opening the switch to the right of TSa2 and closing Sa1. In this way
the fault at the location between TSa2 and TSa3 are isolated and all the secondary substation have
their supply restored.
Nonetheless, with functionalities of digital secondary substations such as intelligent fault detec-
tors, the fault identification and isolation can be performed much quicker. Further, the remote
controlled on-load switches, a key feature within the digital secondary substations enables remote
operation, aiding the automatic fault isolation and restoring the supply by switching the feeding
direction remotely. As a result, over the past years, the distribution grid’s digitization has acceler-
ated intensely and the number of installations of digitized and automated substations have grown
rapidly. The benefits of digitizing these intelligent elements within the distribution grid are widely
accepted from both industry and academia. Also, best practices concerning the optimal placement
of digital substations within existing distribution grids are being sufficiently investigated.
1.1
Rethinking the distribution grid planning strategies
It is a well know fact that electricity networks need to look into the future uncertainties and chal-
lenges which would impede the development of grid to comply with the future requirements. With
the advent of new technologies such as EVs, PVs, Wind turbines (WTs), the complexities in load
pattern are created and because of this uncertain consumption and generation of energy, it leads to
unpredictable power flows.
Modelling and optimal planning of digitized and automated secondary substation
3
==PAGE:18==
The automation of electricity distribution systems is gaining significance as the utilization of dis-
tributed energy sources continues to expand. Managing distributed renewable energy and emerging
load demands, such as electric mobility, presents significant challenges. While medium and low
voltage distribution grids currently exhibit a high degree of automation, it is essential to ensure
their compliance with strict domestic and European regulations. As a result, functionalities such
as monitoring, protection, and control of secondary distribution substations are vital in distribution
systems. In the context of automation, the primary objective is to oversee the grid’s state and trans-
mit measured quantities. A comprehensive understanding of the grid and controllability through
key electromechanical devices within the grid can facilitate this level of automation [10].
Emerging level of automation in the distribution grid focuses on optimum placement of sectional-
izing switches in the distribution [11], [12]. Although, the outage time is reduced by this process,
these sectionalizing switches are controlled by conventional centralized communication system
such as remote controlled units (RTU) and traditional protection systems and this translates to
higher time spent in the fault isolation process. The benefits of the approach towards digitizing the
substations and optimizing their placements within the distribution grid for a much more optimal
functioning are hardly studied nor quantified. Further, the DSOs have financial and resource con-
straints limiting them from fully digitizing all the substations, so an optimum rollout strategy for
digitized secondary substations needs to be outlined.
A recent study aimed to increase the utilization rate of distribution networks through the use of a
semi-automated secondary substations. The primary focus of the study was to analyze the benefits
associated with improved observability and reliability [13]. One observation of the study was that
largest benefits are expected to occur when the optimal locations for placement of the digitized
secondary substations is prioritized based on reliability level. In addition, the study observes that
although factors such as accessibility to secondary substations, the time required for technicians to
reach the area of fault location, and the network topology have an influence on reliability level, as
quantified by the System Average Interruption Duration Index (SAIDI). However, the number of
customers and failure rate at each line section were seen to play a critical role towards reliability.
Further, the study also observed that optimal location to place the digitized secondary substation
was in the middle of the feeder when there were no digitized substations in the feeder and for
placing 2 digitized substations in the feeder simultaneously, the best possible locations included
one substation in the first one-third of the substations in the feeder and the second substation in
the last one-third of the substations in the feeder. Also, it was observed that nodes with three legs
(secondary substation with 3 legs) when chosen were more reliable as three-legged nodes divide
the feeder into two sub- networks, thereby improving both the observability and reliability. An-
other interesting observation was to have a minimum of two nodes between two digital secondary
substations. One major future improvement that was proposed in the study was to investigate the
impact of number of customers at each node and failure rates of line section. While this study pro-
vides a useful summary and general guideline for determining the optimal location for a digitized
secondary substation in a feeder, there are still several questions that remain unanswered such as:
• How does the emergence of new renewable technologies affect the recommended optimal loca-
tion for installing a digitized secondary substation?
• Given the increasing penetration of renewable energy sources, which grid type should be given
priority when placing a digitized secondary substation?
When faced with a large number of medium voltage grids, prioritizing which grids to automate first
becomes essential. In a national projection for distributed generation and consumption proposed in
4
Modelling and optimal planning of digitized and automated secondary substation
==PAGE:19==
[14], it is suggested that the new customer/generation will not be evenly distributed across different
grid types. As a result, the question of which grids should be given priority for automation becomes
even more pressing. In [15], a methodology to identify suitable grids for smart grid automation is
proposed. It focuses on cluster, relevance and observability analysis to identify the grid area and
ranking them according to their priority. A primary cluster analysis is done where local grids are
grouped on the basis of the data on their properties from grid operator. Further, grid station with
high impact on observability and estimation quality are identified as a part of relevance analysis and
are subjected to an observability analysis. While the goal of the study is to create a standardized
integration concept for smart grid automation systems in medium voltage levels, there are still
some important questions to be answered while ranking grid types:
• Does the prioritization of distribution grid type for automation change from a reliability stand-
point ?
• What role do grid characteristics, such as MV cable types, the number of MV lines, and the
type and location of load and generation units within the grid, play in determining its priority for
automation ?
1.2
Investigative scope and Objectives for the project
The current grid planning does have a strong focus on automation of grid components for intel-
ligent and efficient operation of the grid. Digitizing the secondary substations and placing them
at optimal locations within the feeders in the distribution grid is at the core of the grid planning
strategies to have an efficient utilization of grid infrastructure and maximise the utilization of as-
sets. However, the impact of complex load and generation patterns on the optimum placement
location for these digitized secondary substation is to be investigated. The fact that consumer/gen-
eration profiles are not evenly distributed as per national projections for distribution networks in
Denmark makes it necessary to investigate the current findings on the optimal location for digitized
secondary substations within the feeders of a distribution grid. Additionally, the prioritization of
these grid types with variable renewable sources and load profiles needs to be studied from a relia-
bility perspective. Furthermore, the impact of grid characteristics such as MV cable types, failure
probabilities, the number of lines, and the location of load and generation sources within the grid
on its reliability and optimal placement location for digitized secondary substations must also be
investigated.
The objective of this project is to investigate the answers to the following questions:
• What kind of grid type benefits the most from the placement of digitized and automated sec-
ondary substation?
• Where is the optimum location for a digitized secondary substation within the feeder?
• In which framework conditions does the optimal digitized secondary substation location differ
from the open-tie point location in the grid?

This chapter summarizes the main conclusions of this thesis. Furthermore, subsidiary conclusions
that emerge from the study that warrant future investigation are also discussed. Additionally, this
chapter highlights the contribution of this thesis, presents the limitations of studies conducted and
suggests possible future directions for the work.

CHAPTER 2
Theoretical foundations and concepts
This chapter presents an overview of the theoretical foundations and concepts that form the basis
of the thesis work.
2.1
DSO’s involvement and roles in grid planning
Main departmental roles within a distribution system operator can vary according to organizations
size, the country and many other factors. However, some keys roles or responsibilities taken up by
most DSOs include:
• Network planning and development:
Considering the factors like load growth, regulatory requirements, reliability of grid and integra-
tion of renewables. Network planning and development wing of the DSO would be responsible
for these actions.
• Network operation and control:
This team is responsible for the real-time control and management of distribution networks en-
suring a stable supply of electricity to consumers. To acquire real-time measurement data, DSOs
use Supervisory Control and Data Acquisition (SCADA) systems. They are also responsible for
handling emergency situations that involve service restoration following planned and unplanned
outages.
• Asset management:
The primary role of this department is to maintenance, repair and replace equipment in the dis-
tribution networks. Some of the main equipment include cables, transformers and switch gear.
Additionally, it is also responsible for management of assets through optimal allocation of finan-
cial resources.
• Regulatory and compliance:
Compliance with industry regulations, standards and codes of practice.
2.2
Reliability Assessment: role in distribution system planning
DSOs are responsible for ensuring a continuous power supply to a diverse range of customers.
However, providing uninterrupted service is not always feasible due to the random failure of equip-
ment within the power system network, which impacts the reliability of the power supply.
To represent how reliable a power supply can be, the concept of system reliability is introduced.
Modelling and optimal planning of digitized and automated secondary substation
7
==PAGE:22==
System reliability is defined as the capacity of a system to perform its intended functions within a
specified time frame under given conditions [16]. There are two types of system reliability: system
adequacy and system security. System adequacy refers to the ability of a power system to meet all
the demands of end-users, taking into account both scheduled and unscheduled outages within the
system. On the other hand, system security is the power system’s capability to withstand sudden
disturbances, such as short circuits or unexpected losses of system components.
Figure 2.1: System reliability
2.2.1
Component reliability
A power system consists of different components such as circuit breakers, transformers, relays
and failure of these components lead to loss of load and customer interruptions [17]. Each of these
components do have a life time and need to be taken into account while considering the reliability of
a power system network. The useful life of a component with constant failure rate can be expressed
as-
E(T) =

where E(T) is the expected life or useful life, λ is the failure rate of component, t is the expected
time for which the component will survive.
Taking into account the failure λ and repair rates µ of all components within a system, the average
power availability is calculated by tracing power flow paths. Subsequently, equivalent failure and
repair rates are determined based on a series-parallel approach of components. In complex systems,
this can be a challenging task. To address this issue, star-delta and delta-star conversion methods
are developed.
2.2.2
Reliability indices
Interruptions and outages can be analyzed using predictive reliability assessment tools that forecast
customer reliability characteristics based on system topology and component reliability data. These
reliability indices take into account both the duration and frequency of outages. By evaluating these
indices, system operators and planners can gain insights into the performance of the power system
and identify areas for improvement, ultimately enhancing the overall reliability of the electrical
grid.
Types of reliability indices :
• Indices for sustained interruptions
• Indices for momentary interruptions

2.3
Digitized secondary substations
A smart or digitized automated secondary substation refers to a substation that incorporates vari-
ous functionalities which might be beyond its primary purpose of voltage transformation. These
additional capabilities of a digitized secondary substation encompass surveillance (such as short-
circuit indicators), autonomous decision - making, remote control, and protection measures. By
monitoring currents and voltages, the system’s visibility is enhanced, enabling secondary substa-
tions to carry out fault detection and location. Moreover, the combination of remote switch control,
communications, and intelligence facilitates fault isolation and service restoration, granting the sec-
ondary substation self-healing abilities. Furthermore, a smart secondary substation also integrate
volt-VAR control and energy storage features [9].
A digitized and automated secondary substation differs from a conventional secondary substation
as it can have some or all of the above functionalities. So, when there is maximum utilization of
functionalities the main components of digitized automated secondary substation include [10]-
• medium voltage switch gear featuring circuit-breakers with IEDs (Intelligent Electronic Devices)
which are able to provide protection and automation logic and receive commands from a control
center. For example, short circuit and earth fault indicators.
• MV/LV transformer with automatic on-load switch.
• Circuit-breakers with electronic relays for state and measurement acquisition at the low voltage
side.
• Communication system with the control center.
Figure 2.2: Digital automated secondary substation [10]
These features of a digitized secondary substation provide it with the ability to perform automatic
10
Modelling and optimal planning of digitized and automated secondary substation
==PAGE:25==
fault detection, isolation and necessary action for optimal restoration of supply as efficiently as
possible [19].
2.4
Optimization techniques for optimal placement of digitized sec-
ondary substations
One of the primary focuses of distribution planning is system reliability. A reliability indice that is
of great importance when it comes to grid planning is the Energy Not Supplied (ENS) for the system.
ENS plays the crucial role of helping the planners and operators assess and manage the reliability
of the grid as it directly reflects the amount of electricity that is not delivered to the customer
during planned and unplanned outages [20]. In addition to acting as as a performance metric for
the grid, ENS can also help and guide the investment decisions and prioritize grid upgrades and
maintenance. The areas within the grid with high ENS can then be prioritized by the grid planners
for upgrades or maintenance.
As already established in this thesis work, future predictions suggest that the integration of renew-
able resources and load patterns at distribution grids will be unequal, resulting in varying behaviors
of distribution networks and supply interruptions observed at each distribution grid. It is decided
that ENS will serve as the primary metric in determining the optimal placement location of digitized
and automated secondary substations within a grid. By using ENS as the main metric, the study
aims to improve the reliability of the grid by identifying areas for digitized secondary substation
placement that can minimize ENS and improve system performance.
2.4.1
Investigating and selecting the best optimization Algorithm for the Project’s
Problem Statement
The prioritization of the distribution grid for automation with digitized secondary substations is per-
formed on the basis of the Energy Not Supplied (ENS) of the designed grid types. To determine
which conventional secondary substation should be prioritized to be replaced with a digitized one,
an optimization algorithm which optimally places the digitized secondary substation in the distri-
bution grid is needed.
The choice of optimization algorithm depends on the nature of the problem and its specific require-
ments. The problem of finding an optimal location for a digitized secondary substation may not
have a guaranteed global best solution, and a local best solution in that case needs to be selected
instead. Additionally, the search space for the problem may be either too large or too small, de-
pending on the characteristics of the grid (e.g., the number of feeders and their corresponding nodes
or secondary substations).
Considering these factors, a meta heuristic optimization algorithm would be a possible choice for
finding an optimal location for the digitized secondary substation [21]. Meta heuristic algorithms
are designed to handle complex, non-linear problems with large search spaces, and can provide
good solutions in a reasonable amount of time. By using a meta heuristic optimization algorithm,
the study aims to identify the optimal location for the digitized secondary substation and improve
the reliability and performance of the modelled grids.
Two optimization algorithms are developed to determine the optimal placement of digitized substa-
tions within the grid. Initially, a population-based optimization technique called Particle Swarm
Optimization (PSO) is developed. However, the search space of the chosen test grid is relatively
small, and because of that the execution time of the PSO algorithm is not found to be efficient.
Modelling and optimal planning of digitized and automated secondary substation
11
==PAGE:26==
Therefore, a second conventional approach was adopted, which involved converting each sec-
ondary substation to a digitized one sequentially and calculating the ENS of the grid to find the
best location for placement of the digitized secondary substation. This method is utilized for the
investigatory study.
While the conventional optimization approach is utilized for this study, the PSO algorithm devel-
oped is functional and can be efficiently used for investigating larger grid types with larger search
spaces. Therefore, the PSO algorithm can be a suitable option for future projects that involve larger
and more complex grid designs, where the conventional approach may not be as effective.
2.4.2
Particle swarm optimization (PSO)
Particle Swarm Optimization (PSO) is a type of optimization technique developed by emulating
how nature works [22]. The diversity in nature and its systems has driven diversity in emerging
optimization algorithms. These algorithms can be broadly categorized into two types. The first
type is based on inputs inspired by biological entities’ behaviors, such as neural networks, genetic
algorithms, and evolutionary algorithms. The second type is based on inputs inspired by biological
systems’ behavior, such as lions, ants, bees, and other swarm intelligence techniques. PSO is a
meta heuristic that belongs to the second category and is a type of swarm intelligence technique.
It involves a group of particles, each representing a potential solution to the problem, that move
through the search space, guided by their own previous best position and the global best position
found by the swarm. The position and velocity of each particle are updated based on its own
experience and the experience of the swarm, until an optimal solution is found.
Parameters
parameters used to model PSO include-
• Swarm size or the number of particles (n)
• Swarm particle S(n) = s1, s2, ..., sn
• Position of the particle (Pi)
• Velocity of the particle (vi)
• The best solution of a particle (pbesti)
• The best solution for the swarm (gbest)
• fitness function (f)
• Acceleration constants (c1, c2)
• random number between 0 and 1 (r1, r2)
• the iteration number (t)
Modelling equations
The PSO algorithm involves two main equations. The first equation, known as the velocity equa-
tion (2.11), is used to update the velocity of each particle in the swarm. This equation uses and

particle’s confidence in itself and c2 representing its confidence in its neighbours. Together with
the random numbers r1 and r2, these coefficients define the stochastic effect of the cognitive and
social behaviors of the particles.

After calculating the position and velocity vectors for each particle in an iteration, the fitness func-
tion is used to compare the fitness of each particle and determine the best fitness. The fitness of
each particle is then updated individually. This process continues for the number of iterations de-
fined for a given problem, and the best fitness at the end of these iterations represents the optimal
solution.

Methodology: Systematic approach
towards modelling and optimal roll out
of digitized secondary substations in
distribution networks based on system
reliability
In this chapter, the workflow for modeling and optimally placing the digitized secondary substa-
tion in distribution network followed in this thesis is outlined. The step-by-step tasks executed
throughout the process are described. Subsequent chapters will delve into each of these tasks in
detail.
Figure 3.1: Work flow for modelling and optimally placing digitized secondary substation in dis-
tinct distribution grid types
Figure 3.1 illustrates the step-by-step tasks undertaken in this thesis, which are primarily divided
into four parts: modeling of digitized secondary substations and exploring the PowerFactory fea-
tures such as the reliability toolbox and scripting PowerFactory in Python, constructing distinct
distribution grid types, developing an optimal digitized secondary substation placement algorithm,
14
Modelling and optimal planning of digitized and automated secondary substation
==PAGE:29==
and investigating and analyzing the observations out of running the designed optimization algo-
rithm.
1. Design of digitized secondary substation template in PowerFactory:
In this phase of the work, a model for the digitized secondary substation is developed. The
process began with an exploration of the available substation templates in PowerFactory’s
drawing toolbox and an examination of how they could be configured to create a model
suitable for study. Additionally, the study sought to incorporate remote-control switching
capabilities for the secondary substation model. The switch configurations provided by Pow-
erFactory for reliability assessment purposes are thoroughly examined. The “configuring
switches” section within the reliability assessment chapter of the PowerFactory user manual
serve as the primary resource for this aspect of the work [23].
2. Reliability assessment in PowerFactory:
A thorough understanding of the reliability assessment toolbox offered by PowerFactory is
conducted. The procedure for network set up for reliability assessment and the reliability
assessment methodology followed by PowerFactory is studied in detail.
3. Selecting a test grid to apply the modelled secondary substation templates:
A test distribution grid is selected to incorporate the modelled secondary substation templates.
A limited Radius Elnet A/S network area is chosen for the implementation and analysis of
the modelled secondary substations [24].
4. Scripting power factory in Python for easy access and configuration of the test grid:
Owing to the complexity of configuring the parameters of elements within the grid, a general-
purpose language like Python is utilized for parameter configuration and command execution
within PowerFactory. The basic scripting guide for PowerFactory in Python, along with the
Python references document provided by PowerFactory, serve as a handbook for coding in
Python [7]. Additionally, Python facilitate the ease of performing reliability assessment of
networks, storing and analysing the result.
5. Creating load and generation time characteristics profile:
Load and generation are time-varying, so it is necessary to investigate the possibility of in-
corporating these states into the test grid. The “parameter characteristics” chapter within
PowerFactory’s user manual serve as the primary study material for this task [25]. In this
thesis, characteristic profiles are created to represent seasonal variations in load and genera-
tion.
6. Creating distribution grid type scenarios for investigation of optimal placement loca-
tion of digitized secondary substations within the feeders:
The primary focus of this thesis revolves around the impact of digitized secondary substation
placement in various distribution grid types. National projections for future distribution net-
works in Denmark is studied and used as inspiration to design grid types for investigating the
impact and optimal placement location of digitized secondary substations within the feeders
[26]. Mainly, three grid types are designed for evaluation: city, suburban, and energy island
type grids. The energy island type grid is a hypothesized future grid scenario characterized
by a significant penetration of large-scale renewable energy sources. The idea behind en-
Modelling and optimal planning of digitized and automated secondary substation
15
==PAGE:30==
ergy island grid types is to observe the impact of large renewable energy penetration in the
optimal secondary substation placement location within the feeders.
7. Developing an algorithm to find the optimum placement location for digitized sec-
ondary substation within the feeder:
At this stage, various optimization algorithms suitable for identifying the optimal placement
location for digitized secondary substations are explored. Initially, algorithms such as Par-
ticle Swarm Optimization (PSO) and Gradient Descent (GD) Optimization are investigated.
The findings indicated that both gradient descent and particle swarm optimization are more
effective if the search space (number of substations within the feeders) is large enough to ren-
der conventional approaches inefficient and time-consuming. However, for the limited area
distribution network selected for this study, the aforementioned algorithms are less efficient.
As a result, a conventional approach of sequentially converting each secondary substation
within a feeder to a digital one is adopted to determine the optimal placement location for the
digitized secondary substation. Additionally, a PSO algorithm capable of performing the op-
timization is designed. This PSO algorithm can be beneficial for finding optimal placement
locations when future enhancements to the distribution grid are implemented (e.g., when a
large number of substations are present in a feeder).
8. Analysis of the outcomes of optimal placement algorithm:
This stage of the thesis involves analyzing key observations and trends in the optimal place-
ment patterns for digitized secondary substations within the designed distinct grid types,
considering the limitations and assumptions of their design. A ranking system is established
to evaluate the relative benefits of choosing a specific grid type for implementing digitized
secondary substations. The purpose of this ranking is to provide a qualitative approach or
starting point for the DSO to prioritize a particular grid type for automation through digitiz-
ing its secondary substations.
These observations serve as suggestions for the DSO to prioritize and decide on a more
in-depth examination of the benefits of digitizing specific grid types from the various grid
types under their control and supervision. This approach can help the DSO make more
informed decisions about which grid types to focus on for automation and digitization efforts.
Moreover, it is emphasized that the findings or observations should not be considered as
definitive conclusions or references for blindly determining the optimal placement location
for digitized secondary substations. Instead, these insights can be seen as intriguing trends
that warrant further investigation.
9. Sensitivity analysis on key influential drivers for the designed grid
In this section, a sensitivity analysis is carried out on the distinct grid types, considering
their limitations and key assumptions. The analysis assesses the effects of the key influenc-
ing factors defined during the design of these grid type scenarios. This evaluation aids in
understanding the robustness and adaptability of the grid types in response to changes in
the underlying assumptions and driving forces. The findings from the sensitivity analysis
can serve as an impetus for the DSO to thoroughly examine the impact or influence of these
drivers when prioritizing grid types for the placement of digitized secondary substations for
automation purposes.

CHAPTER 4
Implementation of modelling and
optimal placement of digitized
secondary substation based on system
reliability studies
This section provides an overview of the first phase of the work, which involved modeling a con-
ventional and digitized secondary substation, exploring the reliability assessment toolbox offered
by PowerFactory and scripting PowerFactory in Python.
4.1
Secondary substation model in PowerFactory
To create secondary substations (element type: ElmTrfstat) in PowerFactory, predefined templates
are utilized. For this project, a simple low voltage (LV) distribution secondary substation template
from PowerFactory is customized to create a new template. Graphical representations of these
secondary substation templates are available in three types: design view, detailed graphic, and
composite node (beach ball). In this thesis, the design view is used to display the design of the
secondary substation templates, while the beach ball view is utilized to illustrate the connections
between these secondary substation templates within the distribution network. The different types
of graphical representations for the substations are shown in figure 4.1. The drawing toolbox in
PowerFactory provides secondary substation templates under the busbar systems category. In this
project, a modified version of the secondary substation (LV distribution) template from the library
of busbar systems is utilized. The modification involved creating a three-leg secondary substation
template.
Figure 4.1: Graphical representation of substation view [25]
Modelling and optimal planning of digitized and automated secondary substation
17
==PAGE:32==
4.1.1
Assumptions and limitations of secondary substation models
The primary focus of the study is to propose an optimal placement strategy for digitized secondary
substations in various distribution grid types, with a particular emphasis on reliability rather than
observability. Additionally, help to prioritise the distribution network for placement of digitized
secondary substations. The goal of this placement strategy is to facilitate optimal network reconfig-
uration during fault conditions, minimizing energy not supplied to customers and restoring supply
as quickly as possible. To achieve this, the model is designed with the capability to perform fault
isolation and optimal network reconfiguration. The reliability assessment toolbox offered by Pow-
erFactory provides several capabilities and features that are considered while designing the model.
To model the secondary substations effectively, certain necessary assumptions have been made.
Assumptions for the model:
• It is assumed that the secondary substations have all the necessary measurement and protection
devices for its operation.
• The secondary substations are assumed to have all necessary device for communication with
control center.
• The sectionalizing switches within the secondary substations are also protection devices opted
to take part in fault clearance operations.
Limitations of the model:
• No explicit intelligent electronic devices (IEDs) or fault indication devices, such as short circuit
indicators, have been modelled for the secondary substations.
• Only line failure types have been considered and modelled.
• Fault occurrences are considered only on the medium voltage (MV) lines between the nodes.
• No device for communication with control center has been modelled.
4.1.2
Conventional secondary substation model
Figure 4.2 show a simple conventional secondary substation template realized in power factory
[25]. It is a 3 leg substation model with incoming feed from one leg and two other legs for the
outgoing feed. Each leg consists of a manual sectionalizing switch (highlighted in red) which can
be operated for fault clearing and power restoration process. The feed from leg one in bay area 3
is stepped down from 10 kV to 0.4 kV with the help of a 2-winding transformer to a low voltage
(LV) busbar. Further loads are then connected to the LV busbar. A general load element (ElmLod)
is utilized to design the loads in the template.
Figure A.1 in Appendix, illustrates the switch configuration options available in PowerFactory for
conducting reliability assessments. In this configuration, the switch is used as a protective device
during fault clearance operations. Additionally, the switches are manually operated sectionalizing
switches with an actuation time of 45 minutes for power restoration operations. The choice of 45
minutes as an appropriate actuation time was fixed based on a study conducted on smart distribution
transformer substations [9]. Failure data for protection is not considered in the scope of this study.

Figure 4.2: Design view of conventional secondary substation template
4.1.3
Digitized secondary substation model
A digitized secondary substation model is designed, which is represented by the design view shown
in Figure 4.3. The digitized secondary substation has the same 3-leg design as a conventional sec-
ondary substation, with one leg for infeed and two legs available for outfeed. The only difference
between a digitized secondary substation and a conventional secondary substation is that the sec-
tionalizing switches in a digitized secondary substation can be remotely operated.
Figure A.2 illustrates how the sectionalizing switches are designed to be globally controllable dur-
ing fault separation and power restoration operations. The default remote control switch actuation
time set by PowerFactory, which is 1 minute, is utilized.

4.2
Reliability assessment in PowerFactory
Probabilistic network reliability assessment is performed using the reliability assessment toolbox
offered by PowerFactory. The Reliability Assessment procedure considers the network topology,
protection systems, constraints and stochastic failure and repair models to generate reliability in-
dices.
4.2.1
Network set up for reliability assessment
The fundamental procedure to be followed for network setup prior to conducting a reliability as-
sessment in PowerFactory is illustrated in Figure 4.4. Some of these steps are mandatory, while
others are optional before executing the reliability assessment from the reliability toolbox offered
by PowerFactory. The network set up procedure performed as a part of this study is explained in
detail in the following sections. This study focuses solely on stochastic line failure modeling as a
step in the failure modeling process as this is one the most common type of failures observed and
no regular preventive routine maintenance happens [27]. Earth fault situations are not modelled.
Figure 4.4: Procedure for Setting Up Network for Reliability Assessment
20
Modelling and optimal planning of digitized and automated secondary substation
==PAGE:35==
Mandatory set up of network model for reliability assessment:
Before initiating a reliability assessment calculation, it is essential to set up the network model
with the required reliability data models.
• Define stochastic failure and repair models-
Stochastic failure models are used to determine the likelihood of a component’s failure and the
average time needed for its repair. PowerFactory supports various types of stochastic failure mod-
els. Since line failures are the only type of failure considered in this thesis, the line/cable/branch
stochastic model (‘StoTyplne’) is used. This model allows for defining failure frequency (1/k-
m/year) and repair duration (hours) for sustained failures and transient fault frequency (1/k-
m/year) for transient faults. Additionally, it also allows for modeling double earth faults. To
model failures for lines, the failure frequency and repair duration fields are utilized.
• Define radial feeders-
When carrying out a reliability calculation command using the Distribution option referring to
distribution network in ‘Basic Options’ within PowerFactory, it is necessary for the model to
have defined feeders. The feeders can be easily defined by right clicking on the cubicle at the
head of a feeder and selecting define feeder option. Cubicles are used to define the connection
between an edge element to a terminal.
• Configure switches-
Switches in PowerFactory are classified into five categories such as circuit breakers, disconnec-
tors, load-break switch, switch disconnector and disconnecting circuit breaker. For reliability
these switches mainly offer options to specify its behavior for fault clearance operation, fault
separation/power restoration and failure data for protection device. The usage of these configu-
ration options in the context of this thesis are described in the following chapters.
• Define loads-
Load modelling option gives option to specify the number of customers for a load, classify the
load and specify load shedding and transfer parameters.
Optional set up of network model for reliability assessment:
In this thesis, two types of characteristic profiles are used to define the seasonal time-varying load
and generation unit profiles. These profiles are the seasonal profile characteristics (.ChaProfile)
and time characteristics (.ChaTime). A detailed explanation of these profiles is provided in the
following sections.
4.2.2
Reliability analysis procedure
Figure 4.5 shows a basic flow diagram for reliability assessment in PowerFactory [23]. The steps
involved in the reliability assessment include-
• Failure modelling:
The failure modelling involves defining how the system can fail, system failure frequencies and
their repair duration. This thesis primarily focuses on improving reliability during line failures,
and therefore only models line failures for medium voltage (MV) lines in a distribution network.
• Load modelling:
It consists of defining the load demands. It can also be based on a user defined load forecast and
growth scenarios. In this thesis, profile characteristics are utilized to model seasonal hourly load
demands.
Modelling and optimal planning of digitized and automated secondary substation
21
==PAGE:36==
Figure 4.5: Flow diagram for reliability assessment
• System state generation:
The combinations of one or more simultaneous faults and specific load conditions is referred to
as system state. The defined load and failure models are used to generate various system states.
• Failure effect analysis:
It involves analysis of a number of post-fault operational states which includes tripping of break-
ers or fuses for fault clearance, power restoration through closing normally open switches, recon-
necting busbars in substations or establishing a backward recovery, alleviation of overloading
and voltage level violations through load transfer, load shedding and busbar transfer.
• Statistical analysis:
The results of the FEA are combined with data from generated system states to create reliability
statistics which include the reliability indices.
• Reporting:
Power factory by default displays a summary report in the output window. The detailed report
can also be generated with details on failure contingencies, contribution of components classes
to reliability, load interruptions and node interruptions.

Reliability assessment and verification of designed secondary sub-
stations in a test Grid
Figure 4.6 illustrates the layout of the two test grids with their respective feeders and secondary
substations. In this study, two test grids are constructed, each comprising five secondary substa-
tions, to perform reliability assessment. The modelled secondary substation templates are utilized
to construct these test grids. The grids consisted of two feeders: feeder 1 (depicted in blue) and
feeder 2 (depicted in red), with an open tie point at secondary substation 3 (sub3). These test grids
are used to examine the reliability assessment process conducted by PowerFactory and to investi-
gate the impact of placing a digitized secondary substation on the network’s reliability. In Figure
4.6a, the test grid consists only of conventional secondary substations, while in Figure 4.6b, the
test grid has one digitized secondary substation located in the middle (sub3).

4.3.1
Network setup and execution of reliability assessment
The following steps are followed to set up the test grid for reliability assessment. For this example,
only the mandatory setup of the network model for reliability assessment are performed.
• Once the electrical network for the test grid is constructed, the stochastic line failure types for
the medium voltage (MV) lines in the test grid are defined. The line type failure is defined with
a failure frequency of 0.159 times/km/year, and the repair duration is chosen as 6.9 hours [28].
Furthermore, all lines are assumed to be 1 km in length. The line failure model is shown in figure
A.3 in the Appendix.
• The next step is to define the radial feeders, which is a mandatory procedure for the distribution
network-based reliability assessment option in PowerFactory. The radial feeders (feeders 1 and
2 in this case) are defined at the cubicle, as shown in Figure 4.6.
• Next, the switches are configured for both the conventional and digitized secondary substations
and can be observed in the Appendix in figures A.1 and A.2, respectively.
• To model a load, a simple general load element (ElmLod) with an active power of 100 kW is
Modelling and optimal planning of digitized and automated secondary substation
23
==PAGE:38==
defined at the low voltage (LV) side of the secondary substations.
• For the reliability assessment, the option to automatically create contingencies is selected. In
PowerFactory, reliability assessment uses contingency evaluation, where it is not necessary to
pre-define the outage events. Instead, the tool has the capability to automatically create outages
as per the network structure and failure models defined. This process is referred to as contingency
creation. Since line failure types are the only failure type modelled, the reliability assessment
generates a contingencies for each MV line in the network.
• In the reliability assessment toolbox, some basic settings for the network are specified. The
calculation type is set to AC load flow, positive sequence, and the method is set to load flow
analysis. The calculation time period is set to a complete year, and the network type is set to
distribution (optimal power restoration). Under the protection page, switches with protection
devices are chosen for fault clearance. The reliability toolbox with the above mentioned settings
is shown under section A.3 in the Appendix.
• The reliability assessment is finally executed and reported as shown under section A.4 in the
Appendix.
4.3.2
Reliability assessment results
PowerFactory employs a system state enumeration methodology to analyse all the possible system
states sequentially. It uses a fast topological method which means that each possible system state
is only getting analysed once. The frequency of occurrence of a particular state also known as the
state frequencies are calculated as the transition from a healthy state to an unhealthy state and back
again. Also, the individual system states are analysed one after the other chronologically and the
connection between them is lost.
This methodology is quite useful for quick investigations of large distribution system without com-
promising on its accuracy. Additionally, the state enumeration algorithm include possibilities for
including independent failure, simultaneous (n-2) failures, common mode failures, numerous load
states and planned outages. In the reliability assessment that was performed on the test grid only
independent failures have been considering. However, in the following chapters, load states have
been additionally incorporated while performing reliability assessment. The details steps and flow
diagram for the state enumeration process can be viewed under the reliability assessment chapter
in the PowerFactory user manual [25].

in section A.4 in the Appendix is validated. Firstly, the customer interruption duration due to fault
in particular lines are calculated and tabulated. Then the Energy not Supplied (ENS) for the test
grid is calculated by multiplying the customer interruption time with the power interruption (100
kW in this case).
Customer interruption can be calculated as per the equation 4.1. A sample calculation for the
customer interruption at line (line a) in a test grid A and the ENS at substation 1 due to failure at
“line a” is shown below.
customer interruption = failure freq ∗interrupted time ∗customers interrupted
(4.1)
Using equation 4.1, the customer interruption for line (line a) in test grid is calculated where cus-
tomer interruption is 0.159 times/km/annum, the interruption time is 45 mins (0.75 hours) and the
number of customers interrupted during a fault at (line a) are 3 ( sub 1, sub2 and sub3).
Customer interruption (line a) = 0.159 ∗0.75 ∗3 = 0.35775 h/a
Further, multiplying the customer interruption with the power disconnected (100kW) would give
the ENS as 35.775 kWh. Similarly, the ENS for all the lines can be calculated and summed up to
get a total ENS of 0.107 MWh for the test grid A. The same logic applies to test grid B as well.
4.4
Applying secondary substation templates to a test distribution
grid
In order to analyze the optimal locations for placing digitized secondary substations in distribution
networks and examine which grid types to give the highest priority, a limited area Radius distribu-
tion network [24] is chosen as the base network in this thesis. The developed secondary substation
models are applied to the Radius network, which consists of four feeders connected to the primary
bus bar substation which is assumed in this case to be connected to an external grid. These feeders
are interconnected through open tie point connections, and the nominal voltage of the grid is 10
kV. Figure 4.7 represents the radius distribution grid chosen to be the base distribution network for
study in PowerFactory. The modelled secondary substation templates are utilized to develop this
grid. It is assumed that the 4 distribution feeders with secondary substations are capable of stepping
down the voltage from 10 kV to 0.4 kV and supply the two element loads defined (ElmLod) at the
LV distribution side. Additionally, the distance between each substation or the MV line length is
set to a constant 1 km and the MV line voltage is set to 10kV. The specific details on the substation
layout in each feeder are described in table 4.2.

Feeder network topology:
The following are the descriptions of the feeders and open tie point connections in the test distri-
bution network:
• Feeder 1: consists of 15 substations in total and its neighbouring feeders are feeder 2 and 3. It is
connected to the neighbouring feeders through open tie points at substations 23 and 4 respectively.
• Feeder 2: consists of 19 substations in total and its neighbouring feeders are feeder 1 and 4. It
is connected to the neighbouring feeders through open tie points at substation 23 and 25 respec-
tively.
• Feeder 3: consists of 14 substations in total and its neighbouring feeders are feeder 1 and 4. It
connected to feeder 1 through open tie point at substation 4 and connected to feeder 4 through
open tie point at substations 49 and 46 respectively.
• Feeder 4: consists of 15 substations in total and its neighbouring feeders are feeder 2 and feeder
3. It is connected to feeder 2 through open tie point at substation 63 and connected to feeder 3
through open tie points at substations 49 and 46 respectively.
Figure 4.7: Limited area Radius network
The network shown in figure 4.7 is constructed in PowerFactory using elements from the drawing
toolbox. First, a primary bus bar substation (ElmSubstat) from the busbar system is placed as the
primary substation and connected to an external grid source. The defined secondary substation
template (ElmTrfstat), modelled in this study, is accessed from the busbar systems and placed on
the diagram page. Lines from the drawing toolbox are used to create connections among the sub-
stations, and appropriate cable types are assigned later. To define the feeder networks, the cubicle
(connecting an edge element to a busbar) at the starting line of each feeder is right-clicked and
define a feeder is chosen. PowerFactory then automatically defines the feeder based on reaching
an open tie point connection or terminates the feeder when encountering higher voltage levels.

4.5
Scripting Power Factory in python for test grid access and con-
figuration
This section provides a guide on how to integrate PowerFactory with Python and discusses the
benefits that such integration can offer. Python is a general-purpose, high-level programming lan-
guage that is well-suited for handling PowerFactory. To access the functionality of PowerFactory
from Python, a dynamic Python module called “powerfactory.pyd” is used, which interacts with
the PowerFactory Application Programming Interface (API). This module provides Python scripts
with access to an extensive range of data in PowerFactory, including all objects, attributes (such
as element, type and result data), and commands (such as load flow calculation, reliability assess-
ment toolbox etc). Either through the Python command (ComPython) or externally (opening Pow-
erFactory in non-interactive mode using python in interactive computing platforms such as Jupyter
notebook) these dynamic modules can be executed within PowerFactory using python. Leveraging
Python’s power features can greatly enhance the ability to work with data from PowerFactory and
perform complex operations and analysis.
Some of its principal usages include:
• Automation of tasks- python can help in performing the automation of tasks such as creating or
copying attributes and its data using corresponding object types, assigning element data (charac-
teristic profiles for elements) and also accessing all the configuration options within PowerFac-
tory.
• Create functions to perform calculation commands- In this study the reliability assessment for the
network is performed by calling the calculation command using the reliability assessment object
(ComRel3). A function has been written to perform the reliability assessment and is explained
further in this section.
• integrating Power Factory in other applications- PowerFactory offers options to interface with
other applications for exchange of data.
For the thesis, Jupyter notebook is used as the Python computing platform and PowerFactory mod-
ule is imported using the following python command-
1
import powerfactory
To gain access to the PowerFactory environment the following commands are used-
1
app = powerfactory . GetApplication ()
Python object of class powerfactory.GetApplication is called an application object and using this
object (app), the global PowerFactory functionalities can be accessed to perform various opera-
tions.
For example, in this thesis the secondary substation (‘ElmTrfstat’) are accessed and configured
using the below mentioned code-
1
Substation = app . GetCalcRelevantObjects ( ’ *. ElmTrfstat ’ )
The list object ‘Substation’ can then be used to access all the substations of type ‘ElmTrfstat’
present in the active study case, operation scenario or network variations can be configured ac-
cordingly.

percent ’ %(name , loading ) )
First, the list of all lines is stored into an object “Lines” using the GetCalcRelevantObjects com-
mand. Further, using a loop, the loading values for each line is accessed using the GetAttribute
function and later printed. The python reference document [29] for PowerFactory lists all the
available commands. This document has served as the primary guide for developing a workflow
to design and configure the test grid in Python for this project.
4.5.3
Accessing the Result object
The results for certain command operations cannot be directly accessed and printed. Only a few
parameters of the calculation objects can be accessed using the “GetAttribute()” function such as
the line loading and rest of them are stored in a result object “ElmRes” after the simulation. There
28
Modelling and optimal planning of digitized and automated secondary substation
==PAGE:43==
are certain functions defined to access the variables from the result object that stores all kind of
parameters associated to an element. Two of the main functions include-
• int Application.ResGetIndex(DataObject resultFile, DataObject obj, [str varName])
• Application.ResGetData(DataObject resultObject, int iX, [int col])
Using the first function ResGetIndex(), the index for the result variable for the calculation relevant
object (obj) of name “varName” is stored in an object. Further, using the ResGetData() function,
data associated to the variable can be stored in an array and later printed. A simple example to get
the Result object and print the results of a simple RMS simulation can be found under the section
A.5 in the Appendix.
