4.6
Developing load and generation characteristics profiles in Power-
Factory
To incorporate time varying load and generation, PowerFactory provides option to define and use
characteristics. In PowerFactory any parameter may be assigned a range of values (know as char-
acteristics) which is then selected based on the date and time of the study case using a user defined
trigger. The value of characteristics is defined by value of the scale which basically sets the value
of the parameter to the corresponding value. Examples for the scales offered by powerFactory
are continuous scale, discrete scale, frequency scale, time scale etc. The scale are controlled using
something known as a trigger which sets the current value on the corresponding scale. For example,
the active power of a load at a particular hour of the day is set with the help of the triggers.
Further, for a characteristics the range of values can be a one dimensional vector with scaling factors
which basically means a parameter is manually modified by a scaling factor or a two - dimensional
vector such as a time characteristics where a parameters values are modified based on time. Ad-
ditionally these characteristics that are created are stored in the characteristics folder and can be
assigned to a single or multiple number of parameters. This thesis considers the seasonal aspects
of load and generation profiles when assessing the reliability of distribution networks. To input the
seasonal time-varying values for these profiles, a type of characteristic known as profile charac-
teristics (ChaProfile) along with time characteristics (ChaTime) are used. The time characteristics
uses a trigger of date/time of study case.
4.6.1
Time Characteristics (ChaTime)
Time characteristics determines the value of the parameter according to the study time (SetTime).
It provides the option to set a recurrence period for the characteristic values such as daily, weekly,
monthly, yearly and none. Further, specify the resolution to hours or minutes to match the reso-
lution of the values that are to be supplied. The values can be input as a table, file, result file or
database (database outside the PowerFactory database). For this project, the values for the time
characteristics are input as a table through Python. The recurrence is set to daily and the resolu-
tion for data is set to hours. Additionally the values are used as absolute and no approximation
is applied. Figure A.7 in Appendix shows an example of the time characteristics (ChaTime) file
created in this project.
4.6.2
Profile characteristics (ChaProfile)
The profile characteristic is utilized to select a corresponding time characteristic (ChaTime) for
individual days, groups of days, and each season. Additionally, the profile characteristic can be
used to select a time characteristic for certain holiday days as well. Furthermore, in the seasons
page, it is possible to define one or more seasons with a start date and month, and an end date and
month. For the study conducted in this thesis, summer and winter seasonal time characteristics are
utilized for defining the load and generation profiles. The summer months are defined as April,
May, June, July, August and September. The winter months are defined as October, November,
December, January, February and March. Figure A.8 in Appendix, shows a example of a profile
30
Modelling and optimal planning of digitized and automated secondary substation
==PAGE:45==
characteristics that are defined for summer and winter hourly load data for a day. The ChaProfile
type file has two ChaTime type files defined for each season summer and winter respectively.
Steps to create and assign values to the time characteristics (ChaTime) object using Python
A step-wise explanation of the method used in this thesis to create time characteristics objects is
shown below. Python is utilized to create and assign values to these objects.
Step1 It involves creating a time characteristics (ChaTime) object, which is automatically stored in
the characteristics folder in the operational library. However, a separate folder of (IntFolder)
type can also be created in the characteristics folder, and in this thesis, such a folder is used
to store the characteristics objects.
Step 2 In this study, the data source is set to table, recurrence is set to daily, and resolution is set
to hours. This is because an average hourly profile for a typical day in a particular season is
used as the load/generation profile.
Step 3 Next, the hourly resolution data to be input is loaded as a dataframe in Python. The hourly
resolution data is then converted and stored as a list. This is because the values field in the
ChaTime objects accept data as a list. Further, the list is assigned to the values field of the
ChaTime object using the command ‘vector’.
The code for the mentioned steps to create and assign values to a ChaTime object is shown under
section C.1 in the Appendix.
Steps to create profile characteristics (ChaProfile) object and assign time characteristics
(ChaTime) object using Python
The following steps outline the process:
Step 1 Similar to how ChaTime object is created, the ChaProfile characteristics object is created
in the characteristics folder using ‘CreateObject’ command. Further the required seasons
start day, start month, end day and end month is set in the seasons page. In this study, the
defined summer and winter month start and end days are defined.
Step 2 Then the ChaTime object is read from the characteristics folder and assigned to the ChaPro-
file object using ‘co_grp1’ command. If there are multiple fields in the profile object, for
example one profile for weekdays and another for weekends, then weekday profile can be
input using ‘co_grp1’ command and weekends profile can be input using ‘co_grp2’.
The code to create ChaProfile objects and assign ChaTime objects to them is provided under section
C.1 in Appendix.
4.7
Creating load states for reliability assessment
When time-based characteristics are established for the feeder loads, generators, or both, taking
into account the changes in demand over different time periods, these states can be included in the
reliability analysis [23]. Essentially, the load demand for a given duration, say one year, can be
divided into various “load states”, each assigned a probability of occurrence.
Since the load and generation units in this study have hourly time characteristics profiles, load
Modelling and optimal planning of digitized and automated secondary substation
31
==PAGE:46==
states are necessary to incorporate the effect of these characteristics in reliability assessment. So,
the load states are created and loaded before executing the reliability assessment. The steps to
create load states are as follows:
Step 1 From the create load states icon in the reliability toolbar, load states option is selected.
Step 2 The year for which the load states are to be created is defined.
Step 3 The interval accuracy is defined. Lower the accuracy percentage, the more the load states
are generated. In this study, the default value of 5 % accuracy is utilized.
Step 4 Optionally the limit to the number of load states and a field to specify minimum probability
can be defined.
Step 5 Finally generate load states command is executed.
A simple example for the load state creation is explained here. PowerFactory calculates the resul-
tant load states based on the maximum value for each load. For example if there are four loads
(Ld1, Ld2, Ld3, Ld4) and only Ld4 has a time characteristics assigned to it. The load interval size
(Int) is determined for each load, where (Int = Lp ∗Acc), where Acc is the accuracy parameter
entered in percentage. For example, if the peak load observed for a load (Ld4) is 4.03 MW. Then
with a 10% accuracy, the interval size is then calculated as 0.403 MW. For each hour of the time
sweep conducted for each load , the load intervals are determined as:
LInt = Ceil(Li/Int) , where Li is the load value at the hour ‘i’. Further the common intervals are
grouped and represented as independent states for the load and then the probability for occurrence
of each load state based on its frequency is calculated as shown in figure 4.8.
Figure 4.8: Load state dialog box[23]
In this thesis, before running reliability assessment for a network consisting parameters character-
istics objects (ChaProfile) and (ChaTime) defined for an element parameter, load states are created.
The load states are either created using load creation sub toolbox within the reliability assessment
toolbox or is directly updated before running a reliability assessment. The load states are then
utilized for the load flow analysis as a part of the reliability assessment.
32
Modelling and optimal planning of digitized and automated secondary substation
==PAGE:47==
CHAPTER 5
Constructing distribution grid type
scenarios and developing algorithms for
optimally placing digitized secondary
substations
This section describes a workflow that facilitates the creation of distinct types of distribution grids
in PowerFactory. In addition to creating these grids, the focus is on modifying them and generat-
ing various network variation scenarios by utilizing different load and generation units and placing
them at various locations within a feeder network in the grid to create these network variations.
This involves using the network variation option available in PowerFactory to create custom sce-
narios for the designed distribution grids. Additionally, Python codes are developed in this thesis
that automates the creation of new derived network variation scenarios from the modelled base net-
work scenario. Furthermore, optimization algorithms are developed to optimally place digitized
secondary substations within the feeders of the modelled distribution grid scenarios, with the aim
of improving system reliability and minimizing energy supply interruptions.
5.1
Future projections for distribution networks in Denmark
In recent years, many countries worldwide have pledged to achieve a carbon-neutral economy
by reducing carbon emissions and combating climate change. To achieve this goal, the primary
power generation methods in various countries are transitioning from conventional fossil fuel-
based sources to more renewable sources. The dependency on fossil fuels for transportation and
heating is being gradually shifted through electrification. However, these changes present signifi-
cant challenges to the current power systems.
Denmark has already published its goals and future projections to national energy consumption.
However, using these projections directly in grid distribution studies are challenging as the new
consumption and generation patterns may not be evenly distributed. To address this issue, a study
was conducted to propose future projections for distribution grids based on the national projections
from the Transmission System Operator (TSO) [14]. In [14], the Danish distribution grids are
mainly categorized as five distinct grid types (‘City’, ‘suburban’, ‘rural’, ‘Holiday cottages’ and
‘Industry’) on the basis of geographic data. Further, a national projection for consumption for EVs
are used and the percentage of projected EV charger types that are distributed to each grid type is
explained. Similarly, the percentage and type of rooftop PVs and large scale PVs are provided.
Modelling and optimal planning of digitized and automated secondary substation

==PAGE:48==

Table 5.1 shows the projection for rooftop PV in various grid types as a percentage of their total
installed capacity. It is expected that the highest amount of rooftop PV installations will be in
distribution grids in suburban areas.

The projections are displayed as percentages of the total number of each charger type expected to
be installed.
As the unequal distribution of renewable sources such as PVs and EVs as shown in the tables
5.1, 5.2 are projected to affect the reliability of distribution grids, it is essential to develop a clear
strategy for planning and prioritizing the grids most affected from a reliability perspective. It is
the aim of this study to investigate and analyze the potential impact of optimally placing digitized
secondary substations in improving the reliability of distinct grid types. Additionally, it aims to pri-
oritize grid types based on the benefits that can be achieved by implementing a digitized secondary
substation to improve their reliability. Therefore the Grid types and their characteristics from the
national projections study for distribution grid is taken as an inspiration to define and construct
distribution grid type scenarios in PowerFactory. Additionally, the projections for the distribution
of EV chargers and PV installations in these grids are incorporated in to the modelled distinct grid
types scenarios.
5.2
Envisioned grid type scenarios for In-depth investigation
Keeping the interest of this thesis in mind, distinct distribution grids are chosen that have varying
properties to represent future projections for distribution networks. The chosen grid types vary in
the type of renewable energy mix, represent different types of customer loads and varying cable
failure frequencies which are some of the factors that influence the reliability of the distribution
grids.
Therefore, three distinct grid types of City, Suburban and Energy Island are visualized for analysis.
The idea was to differentiate grid types based on the customers/ load level at the LV side of the
substation, cable type and the renewable energy mix (PV, EV, Wind turbines (WTs)). Additionally,
34
Modelling and optimal planning of digitized and automated secondary substation
==PAGE:49==
location and concentration of these load and generation within feeders of the distinct grid types
have been altered to create possible scenarios/network variations within the grid types to observe
the impact of the location of load and generation sources in the optimal placement location for the
digitized secondary substation within feeders.

Table 5.3: Overview of distinct grid types
Table 5.3 gives an overview of the envisioned distinct distribution grid types seen from the per-
spective of their load levels, customer type, MV cable types and the type of renewable energy
mix. As illustrated, three main grid types are considered for modelling. First is a city type grid
which is considered to be a weak grid with high failure probabilities for the MV cables (Paper-
insulated-lead-Covered cables), high load levels of Apartment type customer at each substation
and fast charging profile for EVs. Next is a suburban type grid, which is a relatively strong grid
with low failure frequencies for the MV cables (cross-linked-polyethylene), medium load level
of detached houses type customer, home charging profile for EVs and rooftop PV generation for
self consumption. Finally, an Energy Island grid type is proposed to represent a grid with high re-
newable energy penetration, solely for the self-consumption of medium load-level detached home
customers. This grid type is assumed to have rooftop PV installations for its customers, along with
field PV and onshore WTs connected to the MV distribution lines in the network.
Grid type scenarios modelling : general information and assumptions
• The distinct grid types are modelled on to the same Radius test distribution grid without any
modifications to its network structure. This means that the feeders and their topology remain
constant across all defined grid types, with only variations in load levels and the location of
loads and different renewable energy sources.
• The location of the key influence drivers ( customer load level, PVs, EV chargers, WTs) for
distinct distribution grid types are modified within feeders to create a set of network variation
scenarios.
• All scenarios associated to distinct grid types are modelled on the Radius test distribution grid
consisting 4 feeders without any modification to the network structure of the feeders in the dis-
tinct grid types. Different specific regions within a feeder network where each of the influence
drivers can be placed are defined.
• A maximum load level are determined for the secondary substations in their respective feeders,
Modelling and optimal planning of digitized and automated secondary substation
35
==PAGE:50==
such that the line loading limits are not violated in load flow analysis of Radius test grid. The load
levels at various substations have been defined relative to this maximum load level to emulate
the disproportionate distribution of customers and the concentration of load in particular areas
of the feeder.
• Simulation profiles prepared by Green Power Denmark (Dansk Energi) for network calculations
for network companies are used to model the load and generation profiles. Green Power Den-
mark has come up with a transparent method to calculate the national projections and its impact
on distribution networks [30].
• Two types of cables, PILC and XLPE, are used in creating the grid types and their network
variation scenarios. PILC represents old cables with a high failure frequency due to aging, while
XLPE represents relatively new cables with a low failure frequency, as per [31]. This is in line
with the thesis’s aim to examine cable failures as either low or high failure rates.
• Two types of PV generation, rooftop PV and field PV, are being considered for the grid types,
with power generation assumed to be used solely for self-consumption and no energy storage
capabilities taken into account. Rooftop PV generation is assumed to directly represent the power
generated by the respective customers connected to the substation, with the location of rooftop
PV fixed to follow the location of the customers situated in a particular feeder. On the other hand,
field PV is considered to be independent installations used solely for self-consumption purposes
and directly connected to the MV lines at the possible locations defined for them.
• This study only considers home charger and fast charging profiles for EVs. The home charging
profile is defined as a load at the LV side of the substation based on the number of EVs represent-
ing customers at each substation in the particular grid type. For example, in a suburban-type grid,
1.12 EVs are assumed per terraced house customer as per [14]. Therefore, a time characteristic
profile for home charging profiles of the total number of EVs representing the total number of
terraced customers at a particular substation is represented as a load.
• Similar to field PV, onshore WTs are considered for wind energy based power generation and
self consumption. They are manually placed at defined locations in each feeder.
• The distinct grid types (city, suburban, and energy island) are constructed using a combination of
physical network component placement from the drawing toolbox in PowerFactory and Python
code. For instance, the rooftop PV element, representing rooftop PV at the LV side of each
substation, is achieved through Python code which places a PV element at the LV busbar of
the substation. Whereas the PV element for a field PV installation is manually placed from the
drawing toolbox of PowerFactory and located at the defined locations on the MV lines in the
feeders. A similar manual placement approach is adopted for onshore WTs as well.
• Python codes are developed to automate the process of creation of new network variations based
on the base distribution grid type designed. The user only needs to specify the locations within
a feeder where the influence drivers are to be placed.
36
Modelling and optimal planning of digitized and automated secondary substation
==PAGE:51==
5.3
City type grid
City type grid is defined as a distribution grid in an urban residential area with buildings which are
apartment of 3-4 storeys. The test grid from Radius has been modified to emulate the characteristics
of such a grid type.
5.3.1
Necessary assumptions for modelling city type grid
The following are some of the assumptions made to successfully model city type grid.
• The grid is assumed to be located at an urban residential area where the residential load levels at
most substations are consistently high. Further, all the MV lines in the city type grid is assumed
to be PILC cables of high failure frequency.
• No large or small scale PV penetration is considered in this city type grid. As per the national
projections for distribution grids in Denmark, city grids would only constitute around only 3.5%
of the national projections for rooftop PV installations [14]. Further, field PV installations are
usually far away from cities and more placed in remote locations. Taking these into consideration,
it is assumed that in city grid type scenarios, there is no PV generation.
• The national projections for city distribution grids suggests that majority of EV charger types
would be either fast or rapid chargers. In this study, the city grid type is assumed to have fast
chargers placed at the low voltage end (0.4 kV) of the secondary substations.
• Three fast chargers are assumed to constitute the number of fast chargers at a location. Accord-
ingly, the aggregated charging profile of these three fast chargers are represented as a load.
• A high load level is assumed at the substation.
5.3.2
Work flow for modelling city type grid
Figure 5.1 illustrates the steps involved in creating a city-type grid. The steps are sequentially
performed on radius test grid designed in PowerFactory.
Step 1: Creating load profiles for apartment loads
• Green Power Denmark offers normalized daily load profiles [kWh/(h∗MWh)] for apartments
which when multiplied with annual consumption gives the hourly load (kWh/h) per customer in
a day. The shared profile (DEF 1190) with 95 % fractile data for summer and winter load profiles
are utilized to define the seasonal time varying load per customer.
• A high load level is defined as 80-90% of the maximum load level at the secondary substation.
To represent this high load level, fixed load levels are generated randomly within the 80-90%
range at different substations.
• Further, the approximate number of customers which represent the corresponding defined load
levels at each substation are calculated. The approx number of customers needed to reach the
max load level at the substation are calculated from the maximum hourly load for a customer in
a day from the shared profile (DEF 1190).
• The calculation for approximate number of customers can be explained through an example. The
maximum load for feeder 1 for which it does not violate the line loading limits is found to be
0.37 MW through running load flow. The maximum hourly load consumption (winter month) for
Modelling and optimal planning of digitized and automated secondary substation
37
==PAGE:52==
Figure 5.1: steps to model city type grid
one customer of apartment type is is 3.7 kW from the shared profile (DEF 1190) data provided
by Green Power Denmark. Considering the maximum load level to be 90% at substation 2 in
feeder 1 as an example, the maximum possible load at that substation at any point of time is
calculated to be 90 % of the maximum limit (0.37 MW) which is 0.33 MW. The approximate
number of customers which represent this 90% load level is then calculated by dividing load level
(0.33 MW) by max load of a single customer (0.37 kW) and is calculated to be approximately
88. Similarly, for the corresponding load levels that are set at each substation, the hourly load
profile is found by multiplying the calculated number of customers to the load profile for a single
apartment load type customer. The idea behind this approach is to create a combined hourly load
profile that represents the aggregated power consumption of customers or the load level at the
respective substation which can then be directly assigned as a time characteristics to the load
element at the substation. A general element load (ElmLod) is used to represent the customer
load.
• Load profiles for the apartment type customer is represented as an hourly load for a typical
winter and summer day based on the profiles offered by Green Power Denmark [30]. Time char-
acteristics object (ChaTime) are generated for the summer and winter daily load profiles for the
apartment load at each substation. The hourly load profile representing the daily aggregated
power consumption by the customers at their respective substations are assigned to each of these
ChaTime objects. The detailed explanation of the process involved in creating time character-
istics object and assigning it with value is described under section 4.6.2. Further, the code to
perform this process can be seen under section C.2 in Appendix.
• Profile characteristics object (ChaProfile) are created and used to select the time characteristics
corresponding to load at each substation. The summer and winter time characteristics objects of
the corresponding load at each substation are assigned to a profile characteristics object ( ChaPro-
file) which has the capability to combine the two ChaTime objects to create a seasonal hourly
load profile characteristics object. For the ChaProfile objects, months from April to August are
assigned to have a summer load profile, while months from October to March are assigned to
38
Modelling and optimal planning of digitized and automated secondary substation
==PAGE:53==
have a winter load profile. The ChaTime objects created in the previous step for daily summer
and winter profile are assigned to the ChaProfile objects. The detailed explanation of the process
is described under section 4.6.2 and the code that performs this process can be seen under section
C.3 in Appendix.
Step 2: Creating fast charging profiles for EVs
• A 100% fractile flexibility profile data for hourly charging of fast chargers is utilized in this study.
This profile pertains to a single fast charger and can be easily scaled by multiplication to obtain
the combined profiles for multiple fast chargers.
• In the city grid type, three fast chargers are assumed to be located together, and their combined
load profile is represented.
• To represent fast charging profiles, a time Characteristics Profile object (ChaTime) is created in
PowerFactory. This ChaTime object is then input with the flexible charging profile data to define
the charging behavior. Same process as mentioned under section C.4 is used to assign values to
the ChaTime objects.
Step 3: Creating PILC cable types
• Denmark has mixed MV networks consisting both XLPE and oil-insulated cables (PILC) [32].
However, in this study city type grid is assumed to only have old cables. Therefore, a PILC type
of cable (PILC-CU 3x300 11.00 kV) from the DIgSILENT library is selected.
• The overall failure rate for MV oil paper cables in Denmark is found to be close to 0.024 failures
per km per year [32] and was chosen as the mean failure rate for the PILC cables. The repair
duration for the lines is chosen to be 6.9 hours as per [33].
• In an attempt to maintain realistic failure frequencies for PILC cables, a normal distribution of
failure rates is generated for a total of 69 MV cables in the test grid. The mean value of the failure
rates is set to 0.024, and the standard deviation is set to 0.01 so that the failure frequencies of
PILC cables are not too deviant from the average values calculated in the study on cable failure
rates in Denmark [32].
• For the lines in the test distribution grid, stochastic failure models (StoTyplne) are created with
their corresponding failure frequencies and repair duration. The failure models are created and
their corresponding values are configured. The code to model line failures for the MV lines in
the network is shown in Appendix under section C.5.
Step 4: Assigning the load profiles to the element load (ElmLod) at the substation
• The assigning of load profiles to the corresponding loads is achieved through a Python code.
• Firstly, a dataframe with the substation names and the corresponding profile characteristics
(ChaProfile) object names that are to be assigned are imported as a dataframe in Python.
• All the substations objects and the ChaProfile objects are loaded into Python environment as
shown below-
1
substation = app . GetCalcRelevantObjects ( ’ *. ElmTrfstat ’ ,1)
2
charFold = app . GetProjectFolder ( ’ chars ’ )
3
ChaProfile = charFold . GetContents ( ’ *. ChaProfile ’ )
Modelling and optimal planning of digitized and automated secondary substation

5.4
Suburban type grid
Suburban type grid is characterised as an urban residential area with detached & terraced houses.
5.4.1
Necessary assumptions for modelling suburban type grid
The following are some of the assumptions made to successfully model suburban type grid.
• According to national projections for distribution grids, the highest installed capacity of rooftop
PV is expected to be in suburban areas as shown in table 5.1. Based on this, the model assumes
that rooftop PV is installed on detached and terraced houses within the suburban grid [14].
• Projections also indicate that the highest installation of home chargers for electric vehicles (EVs)
is expected to occur in suburban grids as shown in table 5.2. Therefore, the model assumes that
home chargers are installed with detached and terraced houses within the suburban grid. To
model home charging profiles, statistical data is used which predicts 1.12 EV cars per terraced
house [14].
• The model also assumed suburban type grid to contain mixture of cables types (XLPE and PILC).
The feeders 1 and 2 are assumed to have XLPE type cables and feeders 3 and 4 are assumed to
have PILC type cables.
5.4.2

load profiles is utilized [30].

5.5
Energy Island type grid
In recent years, the concept of distributed generation (DG) has become more prevalent. DG re-
sources generate electricity from locally available, preferably renewable sources of energy. Al-
though the amount of power they produce is significantly lower compared to centralized power
plants, DG resources are situated closer to their customers and can even be integrated into their in-
stallations, also known as embedded generation [36]. The concept of energy islands has emerged
due to the increasing prevalence of embedded generation, which is expected to continue in the
future.
This section includes a basic implementation of an energy island type distribution grid, which in-
corporates field PV installations and onshore wind turbines in each feeder for embedded generation
as shown in figure A.13 in the Appendix. While these generation sources are typically paired with
complementary energy storage technologies, which is outside the scope of this study. The primary
focus is to observe and analyze the benefits and impact of optimal placement of digitized secondary
substations in a grid with high renewable energy penetration from a reliability perspective.
5.5.1
Necessary assumption for modelling energy island type grid
• The grid is assumed to have field PV and onshore wind turbine installations on the MV lines of
the each feeder in the network.
• A residential load of type detached houses is assumed.
• The residential customers are assumed to have rooftop PV and EV home chargers installed.
• The grid is assumed to have relatively new cables of type XLPE with low failure rates.
• No energy storage technologies considered for distributed energy resources (field PV and on-
shore wind turbine)
5.5.2
Workflow for modelling energy island grid
Figure 5.3 illustrates the workflow to create a energy island type grid.
Step 1: Creating field PV and Onshore wind turbine
• To model a field PV system in the feeder, a PV system element (ElmPvsys) is placed at the MV
line. This element is selected from the drawing toolbox in PowerFactory and is connected to the
MV line at every feeder through a cubicle.
• A static generator element (ElmGenstat) is selected from the drawing toolbox in PowerFactory
and is connected to the MV line at every feeder through a cubicle. The Plant Category field in
then set to wind to make it a WT element.
Step 2: Creating load profiles for detached house type customer
• The detached house with electric heating (DEF 1220) of 95% fractile data for summer and winter
profile is utilized [30].
• A medium load level 50-60% is assumed for these houses. Random load levels within 50-60%
range is generated and fixed for the substations.

5.6
Network variation scenarios for grids
While the national projection for distribution grid types provides an overview of future projections
for load and renewable sources in typical grid types, it is important to note that these load and
renewable sources may be located in different regions within the feeder network, resulting in certain
areas with a concentration of load and generation units. This more realistic situation highlights the
need to investigate the impact of such scenarios on the reliability of feeder networks. Moreover,
it is crucial to investigate the optimal location and the impact of placing a digitized secondary
substation in these scenarios to prioritize a grid type for improved reliability, as there are various
distribution grid types in reality where the load and generation are concentrated in a particular area
of a feeder
To create a range of network variation scenarios, the possible locations of these influence drivers
which include load and generation units are defined within each feeder and neighboring feeders.
The possible location of the influence drivers are defined for this study as the start, middle, and end
of each feeder and is referred to as a cluster, with variations in these locations between different
feeders. Table 5.4 shows the possible cluster locations that the influence drivers can lie.
The aim is to create scenarios in which influence drivers within a given feeder and its neighboring
feeders are randomly distributed and placed at a distance from each other. It is worth noting that
the scenarios envisioned for investigation in this study are limited to some of the most obvious and
extreme situations. These scenarios are considered extreme when, for example, the load in one
feeder is at the start, and the load in its neighboring feeder is at the end of the feeder. The Python
codes developed in this study can generate various combinations of influence drivers. Due to the
considerable amount of time required to run the optimization, not all possible combinations are
considered for investigation.

a feeder which is fixed for digitized secondary substation placement and is referred to as current
feeder. Further, each current feeder has two neighbouring feeders referred as first and second
neighbouring feeders. Table B.1 shows the location for the influence drivers in the current feeder
and tables B.2, B.3 show the location for the first and second neighbouring feeders respectively.
5.6.1
Scenarios for city type grid
In a city type grid, the influencing drivers are chosen as customer loads and FC (fast charging)
stations. The customer loads are classified into three types: high load concentration, medium load
concentration, and low load concentration. This means that different regions of a feeder contain
varying load levels, and cluster of these loads create different regions of load concentration within a
feeder. These clusters are defined according to the format specified in table 5.4. Only the location
of cluster with high load concentration is shown in table B.1 for visualization. Similarly, load
concentrations for FC stations are defined by placing the FCs in one of the clusters location as
specified in table 5.4. For the code to create new scenarios by changing the location of the influence
driver, refer to section C.18 in the Appendix.
5.6.2
Scenarios for suburban type grid
In the suburban type grid, the main influence drivers are customer loads, rooftop PV, and EV home
charging profiles. The grid is assumed to have the highest load level at a substation to be medium,
ranging from 50-60%. In each feeder, there are two clusters with medium load levels and one with
a low load level, assigned according to the specified format in the table 5.4. Rooftop PV and EV
home chargers are assumed to follow the location of its customer (medium load level location).
For the code to create new scenarios by changing the location of the influence driver, please refer
to section C.19 in the Appendix.
5.6.3
Scenarios for energy island type grid
Energy island type grid is designed to observe and analyse the impact of grids with huge renewable
penetration towards the optimal location for digitized secondary substation placement to improve
system reliability. The main influence drivers for this grid are the position of field PV and onshore
WT location within the feeders. The location of these influence drivers are mainly categorized as
start, middle and end of the feeders. Table 5.5 shows the possible locations for placing field PV
and onshore WTs within each feeder.

5.7
Optimization algorithm development
Two main optimization approaches are developed to optimally place the digitized secondary substa-
tion in the designed distinct grid type scenarios. As it has already been established, the algorithm
is developed to optimize the Energy Not Supplied (ENS) of the network in both cases. In essence,
the objective function for both optimization problems is the ENS value for the network, and the
desirable optimal solution out of this algorithm is the best substation location to be digitized, giving
the least ENS for the network.
5.7.1
PSO based optimization technique
The PSO algorithm for this thesis is developed in Python using the Pyswarm module. Pyswarm is
an extensible research toolkit for PSO optimization in Python [37]. The basic steps followed by
the optimization algorithm are as follows:
Step 1 The algorithm constants are first initialized. The values for the acceleration constants (c1,
c2), inertia weight (w), number of particles and max number of iteration are defined.
Step 2 The solution space, or the search space, for the optimization is defined. For this optimiza-
tion problem, the search spaces are the substations that constitute each feeder network. The
search for each feeder is separately defined so that the algorithm only searches for the best
location for digitized secondary substation within the chosen feeder for placement.
Step 3 The corresponding search space is then chosen based on the feeder name that is input for
optimization. The search bounds for this particular feeder are then set.
Step 4 Random initial positions within the search space are set to each particle.
Step 5 Further, the fitness for each particle is calculated for the current iteration, which in this case
is the ENS value for the grid. For example, if there are 10 particles, then for the current itera-
tion of the algorithm, each random particle, one at a time, converts the secondary substation
location that they represent as their position to digital with the help of a function that config-
ures switches and executes a reliability assessment, storing the value of ENS. The digitized
secondary substation is then converted back to a conventional secondary substation before
the next particle converts another substation to digital and performs a reliability assessment.
Step 6 The secondary substation location and its corresponding ENS value on converting it to
digital and performing a reliability assessment are basically set as the personal best fitness
for each particle.
Step 7 The fitness for each particle is then compared to find the global best fitness or the global
best solution for the iteration.
Step 8 This process continues for the number of iteration defined and for each iteration, the per-
sonal best fitness of each particle and updated.
Step 9 Finally, the global best solution at the end of all iterations is the optimum location for
placement of the digitized secondary substation in the particular feeder.
Modelling and optimal planning of digitized and automated secondary substation
49
==PAGE:64==
Figure 5.4 shows a basic flowchart that illustrates the sequence of steps involved in the PSO algo-
rithm. The Pyswarm code developed for optimization can be found in section D.1 in the Appendix.
Figure 5.4: PSO flowchart
As part of this study, the developed PSO code is capable of optimizing the placement of the first
digitized secondary substation in the respective feeder. Once the location of the first digitized
secondary substation is fixed, the code looks for the optimal location for the placement of the
second digitized secondary substation in the same feeder network.
50
Modelling and optimal planning of digitized and automated secondary substation
==PAGE:65==
5.7.2
Conventional optimization technique
As mentioned earlier, the optimization technique used to investigate and analyze the optimal loca-
tion for placing digitized secondary substations in the feeder networks of the designed grid types
scenarios uses a conventional approach. This means that this optimization approach does not in-
corporate any intelligent-based approach to solving the problem statement. The reason being that
the test grid chosen for analysis in this study is a limited area network with a relatively small grid,
meaning a small number of feeders and secondary substations that constitute that feeder. The de-
veloped PSO algorithm, when applied to these networks, seems to be an inefficient method as the
search space for optimization is very small and does not require an intelligent-based method to
perform optimization. However, it is also noted that future studies may include larger grids, in
which case an intelligent methodology for optimization is required. Therefore, the PSO algorithm
developed in this study would contribute to this.
he conventional optimization technique involves converting each secondary substation in the grid
individually to a digitized secondary substation to calculate the corresponding ENS for the grid.
The steps for this approach are as follows:
step 1 The search space for the optimization is initialized as the secondary substations that consti-
tute the corresponding feeder network.
Step 2 The scenarios name and its corresponding chosen feeder for digitized secondary substation
placement are input.
Step 3 An optimization function is called, which essentially converts each substation present in
the particular feeder into a digitized secondary substation one at a time and runs the reliability
assessment for them one at a time
Step 4 The ENS values obtained from the reliability assessment upon converting each secondary
substation individually to a digital one are stored in a list.
Step 5 In the list, the indexes for the least ENS value and the second least ENS value are compared
to check whether the least ENS value is a global minimum or a local minimum. If the indexes
for the least and second least ENS values in the list are the same, then this indicates that it
is a local minimum, meaning that there are multiple secondary substations that are giving
the same least value for ENS. Otherwise, the global minimum is chosen as the best possible
location for digitized secondary substation placement in the feeder network.
Step 6 In the case of a local minimum, based on previous knowledge of the best possible location
for digitized secondary substation, the middle substation in that particular feeder is set to
be the optimal location. A dictionary with the middle substation location in each feeder is
defined in the algorithm.
Step 7 Furthermore, the second-best optimal location for placing a digitized secondary substation
in the same feeder is also determined. For this, the above-mentioned steps are repeated once
more to obtain the optimal location for the placement of a second digitized secondary sub-
station. This process is carried out by fixing the location of the first digitized secondary
substation before looking to optimize the placement of the second digitized secondary sub-
station.
Modelling and optimal planning of digitized and automated secondary substation
51
==PAGE:66==
Figure 5.5 shows a basic flowchart that illustrates the sequence of steps involved in the PSO algo-
rithm. The code developed for optimization can be found in section D.2 in the Appendix.
Figure 5.5: Conventional optimization technique flowchart
This optimization algorithm is capable of finding the optimal location for the placement of the first
and second digitized secondary substation in the respective feeder.

==PAGE:67==
CHAPTER 6
Analysis on system reliability based
placement of digitized secondary
substations
The distinct grid type scenarios modelled in this thesis are subjected to the conventional optimiza-
tion algorithm developed to investigate the optimal placement location for a digitized secondary
substation within the feeders in the distribution network, as described under section 5.7.2. As al-
ready established, the algorithm optimally places a digitized secondary substation in the respective
feeders of the modelled scenarios and further places a second digitized secondary substation in the
same feeder both solely seen from a reliability respective. This chapter presents the observations
and analysis of the results obtained, viewed from two perspectives. The first perspective is the
optimal substation locations within the chosen feeder for digitized secondary substation placement
in the conceived scenarios for each grid type, and the second is the Energy Not Supplied (ENS)
indice observed on placing digitized secondary substations optimally in the respective grid type
scenarios. The similar trends and patterns in the optimal placement locations for these digitized
secondary substation are presented and analysed. Further, the scenarios are ranked in terms of the
percentage reduction in ENS on placing a digitized secondary substation in their respective feeders
from a reliability perspective. For this study, the reliability assessments of the grid type scenarios
are only performed for one year as reliability assessment toolbox in PowerFactory offers either to
conduct a reliability assessment for a complete year or a single point in time.
6.1
City type grid scenarios
Figure 6.1 depicts the results obtained from the optimization algorithm for the city type grid scenar-
ios. The algorithm places one digitized secondary substation in each respective chosen feeder for
the scenarios and then places a second digitized secondary substation on the same feeder. Figure
6.1a presents a scatter plot of the calculated optimal solutions for placing the first and second dig-
itized secondary substations in the city type scenarios. The x-axis represents the scenarios along
with their respective chosen feeder for digitized secondary substation placement, and the y-axis rep-
resents the substation number for the secondary substations in the grid. Additionally, figure 6.1b
shows an overlaid bar-graph plot of the corresponding ENS values for the optimal solution for
placement of digitized secondary substations. The x-axis represents the scenarios along with their
respective chosen feeder for digitized secondary substation placement, and the y-axis represents
the respective ENS (MWh/annum) for the optimal solutions.

Optimal location for digitized secondary substation placement:
• The optimal location for placement of the first digitized substation in the feeder is generally
found to be the substation with an open tie point connection with neighbouring feeders as ob-
served in figure 6.1a. For the scenarios grouped under feeder 1 for digitized secondary substation
placement, the optimal location is found to be at substation 4 which has an open tie-point con-
nection with substation 44 in feeder 3. For the scenarios grouped under feeder 2 for digitized
secondary substation placement, the optimal location is found to be substation 25 which has an
open tie-point connection with substation 63 in feeder 4. For the scenarios grouped under feeder
3 for digitized secondary substation placement, the optimal location is found to be substation 46
which has an open tie-point connection with substation 56 in feeder 4.
54
Modelling and optimal planning of digitized and automated secondary substation
==PAGE:69==
• No global optimal location for placement of digitized secondary substation is found for the sce-
narios grouped under feeder 4. Digitizing all the secondary substations that constitute feeder 4
resulted in the same value for ENS. The lack of an open tie point location in feeder 4’s design
is likely responsible for this result. Although feeder 4 is connected to feeder 3 at substations
56, 60, and 63, and to feeder 2 at substation 63, the tie point is situated in the substations in the
neighboring feeders. Thus, in the absence of an open tie point in feeder 4’s design, the optimal
placement for a digitized secondary substation is fixed at the middle of the feeder, which aligns
with previous studies suggesting that the optimal location for a digitized secondary substation is
at the middle of the feeder. For scenario grouped under same feeder, the optimal location for plac-
ing a second digitized secondary substation in a feeder which already has a digitized secondary
substation is seen to be the same. This suggests that location of the influence drivers primarily
the load and generation resources within a particular feeder type does not seem to influence the
placement of a second digitized secondary substation.
ENS observed for optimal solutions:
• As illustrated in figure 6.1b, scenarios grouped under Feeder 2 demonstrate the lowest ENS
values for optimal placement of the first and second digitized secondary substations. Specifically,
scenarios CT8, CT9, CT10, CT11, CT12, and CT13 grouped under Feeder 2 show the lowest
ENS. Additionally, it is evident that the reduction in ENS resulting from placing the first digitized
secondary substation is significantly greater than the additional reduction achieved by placing a
second digitized secondary substation.
• The constancy in ENS values for scenarios grouped under the same feeder can be attributed to
the fact that the optimal placement locations for these scenarios are identical. This observation is
supported by the theoretical formula for calculating ENS, which involves summing up the indi-
vidual ENS values during each failure contingency. The ENS can be represented using equation
6.1, as shown below.
ENS = number of failures / km / a
× outage time (mins)
× average amount of power disconnected
(6.1)
As described under section 4.7, the reliability assessment performs the load flow analysis on the
defined load states for the network. The load states created for each of these scenarios are seen
to be the same, meaning the probability of substations experiencing a certain peak and minimum
loads and generation units are seen to be the same. This probably is due to the fact that the
load and generation levels remain almost the same for of each scenarios, only the location of
these loads in the feeders are positioned differently. For example, the load states for scenarios
CT1 and CT2 are shown under section A.7 in the Appendix and it is seen that they both have
exactly the same load states or same probability for a certain load value. The optimal location
for placement of digitized secondary substations for the scenarios grouped under the same feeder
is found to be the same because when digitizing the substation at the optimally found location,
the individual ENS for each of the failure contingencies sum up to be the minimum than any
digitizing substation in any other location. Now since there is no mixing or change in failure
frequency values for the MV lines across the scenarios. So the individual ENS across each
scenario is then dependent on the outage time. The outage time is different for each substation.
However, for the same optimal location, the outage time experienced by a particular substation
across the scenarios would be the same due to having the same optimal network reconfiguration
to restore supply. In this way it can be justified that the individual ENS for each contingency
across each scenario grouped under the same chosen feeder having the same optimal solution for
network reconfiguration will be the same and therefore when summed up it comes up to be the
same total ENS.

• Figure 6.2b reveals that the least ENS value upon the introduction of a single digitized sec-

lowest ENS value upon introducing a second digitized secondary substation is observed for sce-

the grid when the individual ENS values across the failure contingencies are summed up.
• Although the suburban grid by its design includes both PILC and XLPE cables, XLPE cables for
feeders 1, 2 and PILC cables for feeder 3, 4. However, there is no mixing of cable types within
each feeder and therefore no variation in the failure frequency for a particular MV line across
the scenarios. For example, even though the load and generation units position are different for
scenarios S1 and S2, the failure frequency for the MV “line 1” in both the scenarios will be
same because of the same cable type (XLPE). This again applies to all the MV lines across all
the scenarios. Thus, when optimally placing a digitized secondary substation within the same
feeders, the individual ENS values for all the contingencies across scenarios grouped under the
same feeder for digitized secondary substation placement is the same, resulting in the same total
ENS for the grid.

6.4
Comparison of results across all modelled scenarios
Comparing the results observed from scenarios under each distinct modeled grid type can help vi-
sualize the grid types that require prioritization when placing a digitized secondary substation. To
achieve this, optimal solutions for digitized secondary substation placement are compared. More-
over, the benefit in terms of ENS reduction percentage on placing a digitized secondary substation
at the optimal location for each of these scenarios is also compared and ranked. This ranking helps
in determining which of the modelled grid types needs to be given the highest priority.
Optimal substation placement locations
Figure 6.4 presents the obtained optimal solution for the placement of digitized secondary substa-
tions through running the optimization algorithm. To visualize the optimally found locations for
the digitized secondary substations more clearly, scatter plots depicting the optimal solution for
all modeled grid type scenarios are shown. The scenarios are arranged and grouped based on their
corresponding chosen feeder for optimally placing the digitized secondary substation. For a clear
comparison of results, the optimal solutions for placing the first digitized secondary substation are
plotted in a graph as shown in figure 6.4a, followed by a plot for the second digitized secondary
substation in a graph as shown in figure 6.4b. The x-axis represents the modeled scenarios, while
the y-axis represents the substation number of the secondary substations in the test grid. The aim is
to examine if there are any variation in the optimal solution for scenarios grouped under the same
feeder type, as each feeder for a particular grid type scenario has different influence drivers that
were assumed to create them. Additionally, any general patterns or trends observed in the optimal
solutions are analysed. The key findings are as shown below:
• The optimal solution for placing the first digitized secondary substation for scenarios grouped
under the same feeder for placement is observed to be the same across all modeled grid type
scenarios, as seen in figure 6.4a. These solutions are substations with open tie points connecting
them to neighboring feeders, except for scenarios grouped under feeder 4. There is no global
best location for placing a digitized secondary substation in feeder 4, as explained under section
6.1.
These comparisons reveal that across the modeled scenarios, the mix of load and generation
resources do not seem to influence the placement of the first digitized secondary substation.
Moreover, even for scenarios of a particular grid type (city, suburban and energy island), the
optimal solutions remain the same, indicating that variations in the locations of the influence
drivers such as the load and generation units do not influence the optimal location for placing
the first digitized secondary substation.
• The optimal solution for placing the second digitized secondary substation for scenarios grouped
under the same feeder for placement is observed to be the same for a particular grid type, as seen
in figure 6.4b. However, when scenarios of different grid types are compared to one another,
there seems to be some difference in the optimal solution. For example, it can be seen that for
city grid type scenarios (CT1, CT2, CT3, CT4, CT5, CT6, CT7) grouped under feeder 1 for
optimally placing a second digitized secondary substation, the optimal solution is found to be
substation 15. However, when city type grid scenarios (CT1, CT2, CT3, CT4, CT5, CT6, CT7)
and energy island type grid scenarios (E1, E2, E3, E4, E5, E6) grouped under the same feeder 1
for optimal placement of digitized secondary substations are compared, the optimal solutions are
different, being 15 and 2, respectively. These findings suggest that the mix of load and generation
units that define a grid type becomes more relevant when placing a second digitized secondary
substation.

Percentage ENS reduction
Figure 6.5 shows the ENS reduction in percentage upon placing a digitized secondary substation in
the respective chosen feeders of the modeled scenarios. This visualization is crucial for understand-
ing the benefit in terms of ENS reduction that can be achieved by placing a digitized secondary
substation in various grid types. Here, only the ENS reduction (%) on placing the first digitized
secondary substation is projected, as it has already been established through the observation of
individual grid type results that the added benefit of placing a second digitized secondary substa-
tion is low compared to placing the first digitized secondary substation. Hence, in this study, the
focus is on analyzing and ranking the impact of placing the first digitized secondary substation
in a particular grid type. Such an understanding is a necessary parameter input for prioritizing a
grid for digitized secondary substation placement and ultimately improving the reliability of such
grids. The results are shown as two bar graph plots, where the x-axis shows the modeled scenar-
ios, and the y-axis shows their ENS reduction (%). The scenarios are rearranged in descending
orders of their ENS reduction (%) on placing a digitized secondary substation. The first plot de-
picts the scenarios ranked based on their ENS reduction (%) and grouped according to their grid
types, as shown in figure 6.5a. The second plot depicts the scenarios ranked based on their ENS
reduction (%) and grouped according to their chosen feeder type for digitized secondary substation
placement, as shown in figure 6.5b.
• When the percentage ENS reduction for the modelled scenarios are ranked based on their grid
type, it is observed that scenarios of city-type grid give the highest benefit in terms of ENS
reduction (%) upon placing a digitized secondary substation, followed by suburban and energy
island-type grid scenarios. The highest benefit are seen for a set of city scenarios (CT8, CT9,
CT10, CT11, CT12, and CT14) with an ENS reduction of 52.26 %. This observation highlights
the fact that the ranking of the grid-type scenarios is also in the order of the worst type of cables
present in the grid type scenarios. The highest ranked scenarios (city-type grid) all contain old
cables (PILC type) of high failure frequencies, followed by suburban grid which has two feeders
(feeder 1 and 2) with low failure rate cables (XLPE type) and two feeders (feeder 3 and 4) with
high failure rate cables (PILC type). This is further followed by the energy island-type grid
scenarios where all the cables are new and have low failure frequencies (XLPE type). Therefore,
according to the ranking shown in figure 6.5a, a direct relation between the cable failure and
benefit in terms of ENS reduction can be observed, i.e., grids with high failure rate cables give the
maximum benefit in terms of ENS reduction (%) upon placing a digitized secondary substation.
Further, city grid also consist of a higher load level at the substations and therefore it can be said
that a grid type with high load concentration and high failure rate cables provide the maximum
benefit in terms of ENS reduction (%).
• When the percentage ENS reduction is ranked and grouped based on the chosen feeder for digi-
tized substation placement, it is observed that there is no mixing in the ranks among the scenarios
grouped under different feeder types for the same grid type. For example, in the case of city-type
grid scenarios, all the scenarios grouped under feeder 2 are the highest in terms of ENS reduction
(%) upon placing a digitized secondary substation. This is followed by scenarios grouped under
feeder 1, feeder 3, and finally feeder 4. The same order is observed in the case of suburban-type
grid scenarios. However, for energy island-type grid scenarios, scenarios grouped under feeder
1 are ranked first, followed by feeder 2, feeder 3, and finally feeder 4. Therefore, it is observed
that for a particular grid type, the characteristics of the feeder seems to define the benefit in
terms of ENS reduction (%) that can be achieved. However, there are not enough supporting
results to generalize this trend that is observed. Further investigation into the complexities of
feeder characteristics and their interaction with neighboring feeders would be necessary to draw
a generalized conclusion.

6.5
Sensitivity analysis
Up until now, it is assumed that the city grid scenarios represent a weak grid with high failure
frequencies and high load levels, the suburban grid scenarios represents a relatively strong grid
with low failure frequencies, and the energy island scenarios represent a strong grid with low failure
frequencies and high renewable penetration. These extreme grid types remained the primary focus
of the reliability assessment, as the objective is to prioritize the placement of digitized secondary
substations across distinct grid types.
However, the dependency of ENS on failure frequency, has interested to investigate its impact on
optimal placement of digitized secondary substation within a particular feeder network to answer
the question whether having a mix of failure frequencies in a feeder network impacts the optimal
placement of digitized secondary substations and also what benefit in ENS reduction does that
provide. To further investigate the sensitivity of the optimal digitized secondary substation location
to cable failure frequencies within a feeder, this study explores a mix of low and high failure
frequencies for cables within the feeders and this is achieved my mixing PILC cables of high
failure frequency with XLPE cables of low failure frequency. This approach is taken up to create
the situation of having mixed cable types in a feeder network by defining clusters of lines in each
feeder that are capable of having separate mean failure frequencies. Although this approach has its
limitations, the study on cable failures is not an area of focus for this thesis. In reality, cables in a
feeder network are changed either as a part of maintenance or due to permanent failures, resulting in
cables having different failure frequencies due to factors such as age and repair history. This would
result in a section of cables having a different mean failure frequency in a feeder when compared
to other section of cables in the same feeder. Therefore, for the purpose of study, clusters of lines
are defined in each feeder that can have separate mean failure frequencies.
Table 6.1 shows the possible locations where a particular cable type can be assigned to a set of lines
in the feeder to form clusters. Three clusters, start, middle, and end, are defined for each feeder,
with the aim of creating three regions in each feeder with different mean failure frequencies for the
lines.
Table 6.1: Cluster definition for lines in the feeders
Feeder number
Total lines
Line number

Middle cluster
End cluster

27,28,29
30,31,32,
33,34,35

The conceived mixed cable type scenarios for the distinct grid types are presented in table B.4 in
the Appendix. These scenarios differ from the previously modelled grid type scenarios only in
terms of cable types used. The mix and location of load and generation sources defined for the
distinct grid type scenarios are not been modified. A random placement of PILC and XLPE cables
as a cluster in the particular feeder chosen for digitized secondary substation placement for the
scenarios and its neighbouring feeders are done to create these new modified mixed cable type
scenarios for the distinct grid types. The start, middle and end clusters of lines in the feeders are
designed on the basis of the defined format in table 6.1.

6.6
Sensitivity analysis results
The mixed cable type scenarios modelled in this study are subjected to the optimization algorithm
to investigate the optimal placement location for a digitized secondary substation within the feeders
in the distribution network, as described in section 5.7.2.
City grid mixed cable type scenarios
Figure 6.6 depicts the results for mixed cable type scenarios of city type grid. Figure 6.6a presents
a scatter plot of the calculated optimal solutions for placing the first and second digitized secondary
substations in the city mixed cable type scenarios. The x-axis represents the scenarios along with
their respective chosen feeder for digitized secondary substation placement, and the y-axis repre-
sents the substation number for the optimal digitized secondary substation. Additionally, figure
6.6b shows an overlaid bar-graph plot of the corresponding ENS values for the optimal solution for
placement of digitized secondary substations. The notable findings out of this sensitivity analysis
are shown below:
• The optimal location for the placement of the first digitized secondary substation in the mixed
cable type city scenarios does not appear to deviate from the results observed in the previous city
type grid scenarios. The optimal location for the first digitized substation is still found to be at
the substation with an open tie point connection with the neighboring feeder, as seen in figure
6.6a.
• For scenarios grouped under the same chosen feeder for the placement of a digitized secondary
substation, it is observed that the placement of the second digitized substation in a grid that al-
ready has a digitized substation in the feeder is the same. This observation is consistent with what
that is observed in the previous city grid type scenarios. For example, in the case of scenarios
grouped under feeder 1 (CT_mix_cable 1, CT_mix_cable 2, CT_mix_cable 3, CT_mix_cable
4,CT_mix_cable 5, CT_mix_cable 6, CT_mix_cable 7), the optimal location for a second digi-
tized secondary substation is found to be 15 as shown in figure 6.6a.
• An interesting observation is that the ENS values for the optimal placement of digitized sec-
ondary substations vary among scenarios, even for those grouped under the same chosen feeder
for placement. This can be explained as a result of the mixing of cable types in the feeders. While
the optimal location for digitized secondary substations is the same across scenarios grouped un-
der the same feeder, the failure frequencies experienced by the MV lines in each scenario are
different due to random assignment of cable types to each of the three clusters in the feeders.
For example, consider two mixed cable type scenarios (CT_mix_cable 1 and CT_mix_cable 2)
grouped under feeder 1 for digitized secondary substation placement. In CT_mix_cable 1, the
start, middle, and end clusters of cables are of types XLPE, PILC, and XLPE, respectively. In
CT_mix_cable 2, the start, middle, and end clusters of cables are of types PILC, XLPE, and
PILC, respectively. Therefore, different failure frequencies for these scenarios would result in
different individual ENS values at each failure contingency, and the total ENS value would as a
result be different as observed in figure 6.6b.
• This means that the reduction in ENS observed while placing the first and second digitized sec-
ondary substations cannot be generalized to a set of scenarios of a particular chosen feeder for
the placement of the digitized secondary substation. The highest reduction in ENS while placing
digitized secondary substations is seen for scenario CT_mix_cable 25, which is different from
what was observed in the previous city grid type scenarios.

Suburban grid mixed cable type scenarios
• The optimal location for placing the first digitized secondary substation in the mixed cable type
suburban scenarios also does not appear to deviate from the results observed in the previous
suburban type grid scenarios. The optimal location for the first digitized substation is still found
to be at the substation with an open tie point connection with the neighboring feeder, as seen in
figure 6.7a. These substations are 4, 25, and 46 for feeders 1, 2, and 3, respectively.
• In scenarios that include placing a digitized secondary substation in feeder 4, no global optimal
location for placement is found, which is similar to observation with the previous suburban grid
type scenarios and therefore substation 56 is fixed as the optimal location.
• For scenarios grouped under the same chosen feeder for the placement of a digitized secondary
substation, it is observed that the placement of the second digitized substation in a feeder that
already has a digitized substation is mostly found to be the same. However, an exception is
seen for one scenario grouped under feeder 2 (S_mix_cable 9). For this scenario, the optimal
placement location for the second digitized secondary substation is found to be substation 24
unlike substation 29 which is observed for other scenarios.
• Similar to the mixed city cable type scenarios, the ENS values for optimal location for the place-
ment of digitized secondary substations in mixed suburban cable type scenarios vary, even for
those grouped under the same chosen feeder for placement. This is due to the mixing of cable
types in the feeders, which leads to different failure frequencies experienced by the same MV
lines in each scenario.
Although the optimal location for digitized secondary substations is the same across scenarios
grouped under the same feeder, the random assignment of cable types of different failure fre-
quencies to each of the three clusters in the feeders results in varying individual ENS values
for the substations in each scenario and therefore the total ENS is different as shown in figure
6.7b. For instance, the ENS values for S_mix_cable 1 and S_mix_cable 2 are dissimilar, even
though they both have the same optimal location for placing digitized secondary substations in
feeder 1. This is due to the assigned cable types to the start, middle, and end clusters of cables in
feeder 1. Specifically, for S_mix_cable 1, the cable types are XLPE, PILC, and XLPE, while for
S_mix_cable 2, the cable types are PILC, XLPE, and PILC. For example, the failure frequencies
experienced by Line 1 in S_mix_cable 1 would be relatively low, while the same Line 1 would
experience a high failure frequency in S_mix_cable 2. Consequently, the individual ENS for
the failure contingencies of Line 1, will differ between the two scenarios and therefore the total
summed up ENS for the grid will be different.
• In the case of mixed suburban cable type scenarios,‘S_mix_cable 14 (Feeder 4)’ demonstrates
the lowest ENS when a digitized secondary substation is installed, in contrast to the observations
made in the previous suburban grid type scenarios. In the previous suburban grid type scenarios,
the lowest ENS upon placement of the first digitized secondary substation is observed for a set
of scenarios grouped under Feeder 2 (S6, S7, S8, S9).
This highlights that the reduction in ENS achieved from placing a digitized secondary substation
cannot be generalized for a specific feeder type in mixed cable type scenarios within a particular
grid type. The mixing of cable types results in the feeder having different mean failure frequen-
cies, thereby leading to varying levels of benefits in terms of ENS reduction upon placement
of a digitized secondary substation within the same grid type. Therefore, it is not possible to
conclude that Feeder 2 always has to have the highest priority for placing a digitized secondary
substation in suburban grids when there are mixed cable types in the feeder.
Modelling and optimal planning of digitized and automated secondary substation
67

==PAGE:83==
Energy island grid mixed cable type scenarios
• The optimal location for placing the first digitized secondary substation in the mixed cable type
energy island scenarios also do not appear to deviate from the results observed in the previous
energy island type grid scenarios. The optimal location for the first digitized substation is still
found to be at the substation with an open tie point connection with the neighboring feeder, as
seen in figure 6.8a. These substations are 4, 25, and 46 for feeders 1, 2, and 3, respectively.
• In scenarios that include placing a digitized secondary substation in feeder 4, no global optimal
location for placement is found, which is similar to what was observed with the previous energy
island grid type scenarios and therefore substation 56 is fixed as the optimal location.
• For scenarios grouped under the same chosen feeder for the placement of a digitized secondary
substation, a notable difference is observed in some scenarios where the optimal solution for
the placement of a second digitized secondary substation seems to vary in comparison to the
previous conceived energy island grid type scenarios. The variation in the optimal location for
the second digitized secondary substation is especially visible in the scenarios that are grouped
under feeder 1 and feeder 2 for optimal placement of digitized secondary substations. In the case
of scenarios grouped under feeder 1, the optimal location is seen to vary between substations 2
and 3. For scenarios grouped under feeder 2, the optimal location is seen to be substation 29
for scenario E_mix_cable 12, unlike the rest of the scenarios grouped under feeder 2 where the
optimal location is seen to be substation 22.
• The observation suggests that there may be a correlation between high renewable energy penetra-
tion and mixed cable types in a feeder resulting in varying optimal location for placing a second
digitized secondary substation. However, it is not possible to draw a generalization that mixed
cable type scenarios with high penetration of renewables always lead to variation in the optimal
placement of the second digitized substation. Nevertheless, these observations are interesting
and could provide a direction for future research to conduct a more in-depth analysis on this
topic.
• Similar to the mixed city cable type scenarios, the ENS values for optimal location for the place-
ment of digitized secondary substations in mixed energy island cable type scenarios vary, even
for those grouped under the same chosen feeder for placement. This is due to the mixing of ca-
ble types in the feeders, which leads to different failure frequencies experienced by similar MV
lines across the scenario. Although, there are a few scenarios where the optimal placement of the
second digitized secondary substation in scenarios grouped under the same feeder are different
which would obviously lead to a different ENS. However, even for scenarios with same optimal
solution grouped under the same feeder, the ENS values are seen to be different because of mix-
ing of failure frequencies of cables. For instance, scenarios E_mix_cable 1 and E_mix_cable 2,
in both the cases the optimal placement of first digitized secondary substations is substation 4
in Feeder 1, yet the total ENS is seen different due to similar MV lines in feeder 1 one having
different failure frequencies for both scenarios, resulting in different individual ENS for failure
contingencies, leading to a different total ENS for the network.
• In the case of mixed energy island cable type scenarios, E_mix_cable 14 shows the lowest ENS
on placing first digitized secondary substation and E_mix_cable 16 shows the lowest ENS on
placing the second digitized secondary substation as shown in figure 6.8b. This highlights the
fact that having mixed cable types, which can result in mixed failure frequencies for the cables,
may yield varying levels of ENS reduction when one or two digitized secondary substations are
placed in different scenarios.
